"THE BULLETIN” BOOK EXCHANGE.
On receipt at The Bulletin Office, Sydney, of Postal Order, Postal Notes, or Stamps equal to the price stated (which is
published price with postage added), books in the subjoined list will be forwarded to any Australasian address.
SOME RECENT BOOKS.
PHI. May’s Mustrated Winter Annual (Is
3d) comes to cheer the Australian summer.
The pictures are many and clever, and the
prose is up to the English short-story
average. In “ Fun, Frolic, and Fancy,”
by Byron Webber and Phil. May (Is 3d),
tbe pictures are fewer and the writing
duller.
The Autocrat of the Breakfast-Table is
issued by Walter Scott in a cheap and
charming dress (cloth, 2s lOd) —an act of
homage which the manes of the dead
Autocrat, Poet, and Professor will doubt-
less appreciate.
The Goldfields of Westralia (cloth, Is 3d) is
a small handbook of W. A. mining, issued
by Barker and Co., a firm of London
brokers. The news and comment are now
mostly superseded, though the reader is
carried up to July of last year ; but there
are a big map and half-a-dozen sketch plans
of all the leading fields, which are well
worth the price.
Beenes from the Show (cloth, 2s. lid.) is
another collection of G. R. Sims’ stories,
written down to the level of the middle-
class English intelligence —weak in con-
ception and dull in treatment.
A» Introduction to Comparative Psychology,
by C. Lloyd Morgan (cloth, 6s. 6d), is the
latest volume in the “ Contemporary
Science Series.” It is interesting to the
student, but hardly fitted by treatment to
be a favorite (apart from the difficulty of
the subject). The increase of price beyond
the 4s. average has thus no special justi-
fication.
Gould’s Game of Draughts (cloth, 2s. lOd.)
is a handy little compendium of problems,
critical positions and games, which experts
and would-be experts will find one of the
best text-books published.
The Tales of John Oliver Hobbes (cloth, 6s.
6d.) now appear in a very nice edition,
worth buying “ for keeps.” The volume
includes “ Some Emotions and a Moral,”
“A Study in Temptations,” “A Bundle
of Life,” and “The Sinner’s Comedy;”
and comes with a piquant flavor of literary
olives very refreshing to the jaded palate.
J. O. H. has perhaps the most masculine
intellect among the notable woman writers
of the day, and her wit is of a Wilde-ish
deftness which none of her rivals can
approach.
The Manxman, by Hall Caine (cloth, 45.),
is republished in a cheap and readable
form, and can be recommended as a good
and powerful novel of the dramatic class.
Australian Books.
For the Term of His Natural Life, by Marcus
Clarke. Cloth, 2s 9d. The best Austra-
lian novel.
Robbery Under Arms, by Rolf Boldrewood,
Paper, 2s 9d ; Cloth, 3s 9d. The second-
best Australian novel.

Bs.

By Reef and Palm, by Louis Becke. Paper,
Is 9d. Strong and fascinating.

Cloth, 3s. A charming big-children’s book.

3s. The best criticism of Australian char-
acter yet written.
Selections from Henry Lawson’s works.
Paper, Is. 3d. The budding of an Aus-
tralian genius.
The Art of Living in Australia, by Dr. P. E.
Muskett. Cloth, 4s. Practical health
hints, with 300 valuable cookery recipes.
Views of Melbourne. Views of Sydney.
Beauty Spots of Sydney. Beauty Spots of
N.S. Wales. Four small albums, 7in. by
6in., with 12 to 24 pictures very nicely
printed from photos. Cloth, Is 2d each.
Pictures of New South Wales. This book
contains in album form 100 large photo*
engravings of N.S.W. scenery, shipping,
and other matters of interest, and can be
warmly recommended as the best and
most comprehensive collection of the kind
yet offered. As a gift to far-away friends,
it cannot be surpassed. The binding and
finish are excellent. Posted to any Aus-
tralasian address for 5s 8d ; foreign 6s 2d.
Mbs. Rawson’s Australian Poultry Book.
Paper, Is 3d. This book was specially
written for Australian conditions by Mrs.
Lance Rawson, of Rockhampton, Q.
Index Map of New South Wales. 2s 6d.
Measures 3ft. by 2ft. 6in.; folds to pocket
form. Showing roads, caves, tourist re-
sorts, etc.; well up to date.

A new series in course of publication,
beautifully printed and illustrated. Paper,
3s ; cloth, 4s per vol.

With numerous illustrations by Paul

George Moore’s Novels.
George Moore, “the English Zola,” leads
the British school of realistic fiction. All his
work is strong and convincing, and “ Esther
Waters,” his latest book, lifts him into front
literary rank.

Memoirs of Sherlock Holmes, by Conan

The Sign of Four, by Conan Doyle. Paper,

Paper, 9d ; Cloth, Is. The author’s
best book, well printed.
The Cloister and the Hearth, by Charles
Reade. Paper, 9d ; Cloth, Is.
Tess of the D’Urbervilles, by Thomas Hardy.
Paper, 2s lOd. A masterpiece of English
fiction.
Crime and Punishment, by Fedor Doatoieff-
sky. Paper, 3s. One of the most re-
markable of Russian realistic novels.
The Anarchist Peril, by Felix Dubois.
Cloth, 6s 6d. A startling exposure.
Lorna Doone, by R. D. Blackmore. Cloth,
3s. A charming English romance.
Looking Backward, by Edward Bellamy.
Paper, Is 3d. The bane or the antidote.
Pictures of the Socialistic Future, by Eugene
Richter. Paper, Is 3d. The antidote or
the bane.
Geoffry Hamlyn, by Henry Kingsley.
Cloth, 4s. A readable tale'about Australia.
Studies in Pessimism, by Schopenhauer,
Paper, Is. 3d. Delightfully cynical.
The Green Carnation. Paper, 45.; cloth,
6s. 6d. Witty and amusing.
The Human Species, by Prof. A. de Quatre-
fages. Cloth, 5s 6d.
Virginibus Puerisque, by R. L. Stevenson.
Cloth, 6s. 6d. Essays now well-known,
but as fresh as Spring, and perennially
charming.
My Lady Nicotine, by J. M. Barrie. Cloth,
6s. 6d. A book chief among ten thousand
and altogether lovely. The smoker who
has not read it has not smoked.
Noughts and Crosses, by A. T. Quiller Couch.
Cloth, 6s. 6d. Surely the quaintest,
sweetest stories ever penned, with art
reaching its acme in deliciously subtle
simplicity.
In a Canadian Canoe, by Barry Pain. Cloth,
4s. Sketches, veuses and stories varying
in merit, but with keen thought and a
clever style at the back of all.
Prose Fancies, by Richard Le Gallienne.
Cloth, 6s. (id. A recent book of idyllic
essays, throwing the glamour of imagination
over the desert of commonplace, and trans-
muting lead to gold by the alchemy of
exquisite prose.
Post-Prandial Philosophy, by Grant Allen.
Cloth, 4s In these articles Grant Allen
brings his scientific knowledge and scien-
tific trahiing to bear effectively on subjects
of the day.

Blackie’s Modern Cyclopedia.
This is a handy encyclopedia in eight neat
volumes of workaday size. While not so
full or so up-to-date as the very expensive-
publications of its class, it contains an
immense mass of varied information on
nearly all subjects of enquiry, and strikes a
happy medium between the vast encyclopedia
in 20 or 30 heavy tomes and the disappoint-
ing single-volume compendium.
Strongly-bound in cloth and well printed ;
the set of eight volumes for 505., packed or
posted anywhere in Australasia.

Pitman’s Shorthand Books.
Every enterprising man catches short-
hand, like measles, at a certain stage of
growth ; and Pitman’s is, on the whole, the
best system to catch. It is simple, scientific,
and reliable.
The books recommended to the student on
commencing the study of Phonetic Short-
hand are the “Phonographic Teacher,”
“ Key,” and “ Progressive Studies,” or
“Pitman’s Shorthand Instructor” and
“ Key.” The art may be learned from these
hooks without the aid of a personal teacher.
The Phonographic Teacher ; or First Book
in Shorthand, a Guide to a Practical
Acquaintance with the Art of Phonography
or Phonetic Shorthand, containing a Series
of Progressive Lessons. 7d.
Key to the Phonographic Teacher. 7d.
Progressive Studies in Phonography ; a-
simple and extended exposition of the
Art of Phonetic Shorthand, as set forth
in the Phonetic Teacher, Manual of Phono-
graphy, and Reporter, intended as a sup-
i plementary book to these three. Is Id.
A Manual of Phonography ; containing a
complete exposition of the system, with
numerous shorthand examples interspersed
with the text, and exercises in reading and
writing. Is 7d.
The Phonographic Reader ; a course of
reading exercises in Phonography, with a
Key in ordinary type. 7d.
The Phonographic Reporter or Reporter’s
Companion ; an adaptation of Phonography
to Verbatim Reporting. 2s 2d ; cloth,
2s 9d.
The Phonographic Phrase Book, containing:
above 2000 useful phrases in Phonography,
with key and exercise- on all the phrases
(new edition). Is Id.
A Phonographic Dictionary of the English’
Language, containing the shorthand forms
for 55,000 words, and 5000 proper names.
Sixth edition. Crown Bvo., handsome
cloth binding. 4s 4d.
All Pitman’s other publications may be
had on application.

A GOLDEN SHANTY.’
Typhoid is raging at Five Dock, Sydney.
In other words, filth has come home to roost.
The Duke of Argyle is getting ready to
marry a third wife. The duke has done his best
to cheapen the distinction of being step-mother to
the Queen’s son-in-law, Lome, and that poor,
forgotten individual is said to deplore the nuptial
tendencies of his “old man” in a minor key.
This is how the British mercantile class
loves the British seaman. Runciman, the Tyne-
side shipowner, has lately been transferring
several ships to the Norwegian flag. Says he :
“ On a ship of 2000 tons burthen, I thus save <£C2
10s. in wages and <£lo on provisions per month.”
The band will now play “Ye Mariners of
England ! ”
The late Dueness of Montrose had a free
and independent tongue in any sort of company.
Once, when Albert Edward, trying to dissuade the
choleric old girl from scratching a favorite at the
eleventh hour, suggested that their Jockey Club
friends would not like it, she turned her back
on royalty, saying “The Jockey Club can go to —
Rockhampton 1”
From the S.M. Herald ! !
In a, Bookseller’s Shop at Brighton (Eng.). Lady:
“Will you give me ‘The Eloping Angels,’ by William
Watson?’’ Shopman: “I am sorry, madam, we have not
a copy left. Will you take the sequel to it, ‘ The Heavenly
Twins’ ?”
Shame !
Henry Yarley, tue loud-mouthed butcher-
evangelist, has come back to Australia. Yarley
used to be about the coarsest and most aggressive
of the evangelist type, and his addresses “tomen
only ” were among the steepest and most naked
things of their kind ever heard in this country.
Why is it that coarseness and a certain hunky
variety of “ humor ” are always the ear-marks of
the man who calls himself an “evangelist ? ”
From Cheltenham (writes The Bulletin’s
London correspondent by last mail) has just come
the news of the death of my old friend, Mr.
William Watson, brother of Mr. Geo. Watson,
Master of the Melb. Hounds. The late Mr. Wat-
son was a real specimen of an old Irish, or English,
sportsman, and seldom failed to put in an appear-
ance at the Cotswold meets. He was probably
the most popular man in Cheltenham, where
everybody admired and respected him. The
Watsons are a real straight-going lot of riders.
Another brother, John Watson, has been Master
of the Meath Hounds for over 40 years, and is
still hard to follow. A week or two ago he had
a nasty fall and his horse rolled on him, but
happily, barring a broken shoulder-blade, he is
not much the worse for his spill.
When a new Czar of Russia is proclaimed
the masses are promptly summoned to the nearest
police-station for the purpose of taking the oath
of allegiance. As soon as swearing-time has
elapsed the police start on a house-to-house
enquiry, and any person found trying to dodge his
duty is served with a blue notice paper, com-
manding payment of oath arrears within 24 hours.
The penalty of disobedience is a prosecution for
treason. Invalids, who can’t. take up their beds
and walk to the station, are made loyal on the
premises by a vigilant, inspector ; and in cases
where the subject happens to he dying and unable
to articukite, the inspector seizes the cold, limp
arm of the defaulting party and &wears him or her
in by proxy. Arraueement-f fur obtaining an all-
round allegiance are thoroughly* Russian in their
completeness. People are vaccinated, so to speak,
with loyalty to the new Czar, and get no chance
to plead hereafter that they didn’t “ take.”
All the rage in Paris. Latest Parisian
preventative posted in sealed letter, 1/2 stamps.
National Drug Co., Russell-st.,Melbourne.
Ihe soul of J. H. Angas, a South Austra-
lian squatter, is aching on account of two abori-
gines, the last survivinginhabitar.ts of a “station”
that lias been reappropriated by the local Grown.
They were shifted the other w.eek by Act of Parlia-
ment and the Act forgot to flnd’ariotherreslingplaoe
for them. “ The case of these two primeval lords of
the soil is truly one with a barren and hopeless
outlook, and considering the dispossession of
their race, it certainly not only savours of, but
is sheer inhuman,” says this eloquent squatter in
a letter to the local Advertiser. In a sad, re-
proachful tone of voice, Mr. Angas calls upon the
Government to allow his dark and lonesome breth-
ren to choose a block of. the land which is “their
heritage,” where they may live in peace and
security. The Bulletin begs to support the
squatter’s touching appeal. One of the richest
bits of country in South Australia is a trifle of
15.000 acres at the back of Melrose, and J. 11.
Angas figures as the nominal owner of it. He has
another 45,000 acres close handy, but this is land
on which he raises cattle and rents and other
useful things. The smaller block is merely unoc-
cupied soil waiting to be picked up by the first
primeval lord who comes along. Squatter Angas
bought the property from the Crown that stole it
from the blacks, but on his own showing he stands
in the position of a man who buys the proceeds of
a robbery at a ridiculously low price. He took
the risk of a claim for restitution from the rightful
owners, and the blacks aforesaid, being the rightful
owners of Angas’s earth, ought to have those
15.000 neglected acres handed back to them at
once.

while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
19 of this issue.

NOWICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Rost Office Order, One Round
Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, bat is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Maclbod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknoivledge in the
* Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other A rticles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in lilnck and White,
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
ot.ier topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event, will
be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture containing
a good or adaptable idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion—i.e., any picture which has to,be re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.
Copies of The Bulletin can be obtained in London,
after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Co., Limited, 31 Fleet Street, E.C.
Also in the Transvaal : —The Standard and Diggers
News Co., Limited, Johannesburg, South Africa.
Also: — R. A. Thompson and Co., 3 Church Street,
Capetown, South Africa.

and South Australia.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage by the Australian and Tasmanian Governments
on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual subscription
outside N.S. Wales is now £l, with 2s. 2d. added for
postage.
No newspaper can he sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.

SATURDAY, JANUARY 19, 1895.
A Financial Nightmare.
The Victorian Legislative Council has acted,
in the country’s dire emergency, as every-
body who knew it well foresaw that it would
act. The Land and Income-tax Bill was
thrown aside by an almost unanimous vote,
the Governinent representative forming a
lonesonie minority of one in favor of the
measure. Victoria, as the Council is fully
aware, is just now in the direst straits that
province has yet experienced!. The revenue
has "fallen off, in five years, from nearly
£10,000,000 per annum to considerably less
than £7,000,000. To cover the ever-
acfcutnulating. deficit successive Treasurers
have borrowed all the available trust-funds
—some £4,000,000 or more —and it is prac-
tically' impossible to raise any more tem-
porary loans froin this source without com-
pelling the Savings Bank to stop payment.
Retrenchment has been practised on an
enormous scale till there is hardly anybody
left to retrench. The interest on the public
debt has been provided only by the hard
expedient of postponing payment of many
of the country’s just debts, on the under-
standing that the arrears would be liquidated
when the returns from the land and income
taxes began to come in. As Premier Turner
truthfully stated, the province is on the
verge of stopping payment—in fact, unless
some source of income can be foundapart from
the ordinary revenue, Victoria, apparently,
must file its schedule when the next half-
year’s interest to the English creditor falls due.
The proposed land and income tax was the
only promising means whereby the necessary
revenue could be raised, and when the 48
rich, fat men of the Legislative Council were
confronted by the necessity of taxing them-
selves and their own class, or else letting the
country go broke, they decided by about the
largest majority on record that the country
could go broke if it wanted to f ,
There is a calm and hog-like candor about
the attitude of the Victorian Upper House
in this matter which is beyond even the
ordinary record of that body and others of
its species. The reason assigned for
its action is that the land and income
tax is a measure designed for the
spoliation of a class —admittedly its own
class. And so, undoubtedly, it is. All
taxation is spoliation, more or less ; and the
choice in the present instance lies between
taxing the class which possesses available
wealth and can pay, and taxing the class
which lias no wealth and can’t pay. The
Turner Government necessarily chose the
former expedient, but the Council, which
directly represents almost all the accumu-
lated wealth of the province, flatly refuses
to contribute anything to help the country
in its time of trouble. Hitherto there has
been practically no special taxation on wealth
in Victoria. The present land-tax yields
about £120,000 a year out of an aggregate
income which fluctuates between six and ten
millions, and, apart from this, the poor and
the rich pay very much alike. The roads
and railways which have opened up the Fat
Man’s property and added about£loo,ooo,ooo
to its value have been made at the expense
of both classes, but mainly at the ex-
pense of the poor. because there are more
of them. The ten or twelve millions which
have been spent in buying back lands
at enormous prices for railway purposes have
been mostly levied on the poor and almost
wholly paid over to the rich. The poor have
helped to pay for police and military forces
for the protection of the Fat Man’s property,
and in every other way a beautiful equality
has prevailed. And now that the poorer
section of the community can no longer
struggle on under the burden of the vast
liabilities it has incurred;/mostly for the Fat
Man’s benefit, it invites him to save the
country by contributing a little more than
his present share of taxation, and his un-
hesitating reply is that if the country can
only be saved on these terms he is quite
content that it should be lost.
• * •
Under such circumstances, the Turner
Ministry has very few alternatives before it.
It may attempt to save another £500,000 per
annum by retrenchment, and let the present
huge deficit stand over in the good old way ;
but unfortunately no one knows where any-
thing like another £500,000 can be saved
out of an annual expenditure that has already
been retrenched unto seventy times seven.
It may attempt to raise the required half-
million p#r annum by more taxation on the
poor, seeing that the wealthy classes have
given definite notice that they won’t pay, no
matter what happens—but taxes on the poor
hardly pay the cost of collection. Or it may
announce to the British creditor that the
province proposes to compound on the basis
of ss. or 10s. in the £ until times impro/e,
and chat, when the improvement comes, it
may or may not resume payment, according
to how it feels on the subject. A fourth
alternative—that of floating a loan to supply
this year s deficit, and then floating a larger
loan to supply next year’s deficit plus the
interest on this year’s loan, and floating more
loans every year until borrow ing comes to an
end by a natural process of collapse—is left
out of the calculation. Victoria’s difficulty
is its almost total inability to pay out of its
present revenue interest on the existing
public debt, and under these circumstances
the folly of contracting to pay more interest
in the future is sufficiently apparent. More-
over, the trouble lies, not so much in the
want of resources—-for Victoria is still about
the richest province in Australasia—as
in the definite resolve of the propertied
classes, expressed through their own especial
branch of the Legislature, not to contribute,
a copper to rescue the country from bank-
ruptcy. The only hope for th,e immediate,
future is that the aggregate. Fat Man of Vic-
toria will repent of his hog-like selfishness
and assume a more reasonable attitude, and;
this chance i,s so exceedingly remqte th,a,t
to incur new liabilities which can only be
met when the Fat repents, and amends
his way would be a worse form of dishonesty
than almost any other that can be imagined.
# # *
The reasons why the Victorian Democracy
should prefer—supposing the Council per-
sists in its present attitude—to leave the
English creditor unpaid rather than stave
off the disaster by temporary expedients are
sufficiently logical. In Victoria, property
represents three-fourths of the Legislature.
The propertied classes elect the Council
which represents them and their interests
alone. They also, by virtue of the extra
voting power which is conferred on bricks and
mortar, electsomething like half the Assembly.
They hold, and always have held, by far the
largest share of political power. It was the
votes of the propertied classes which piled
up the huge public debt of Victoria. It was
also the propertied classes which controlled
its expenditure. It was these classes that
built hopelessly unprofitable railways all
over the province, that created the hideous
disaster of the boom which is responsible for
all Victoria’s present troubles, that refused
to face the deficit in its early stages—that
did everything, in short, which brought the
community to its present pass. And now
that Property, through its elected represen-
tatives, unanimously refuses to do anything
to meet the emergency it has itself created,
the Democracy is fully justified in letting
things slide. The case for the citizen who
does not own property—the person who
represents two-thirds, or thereabouts, of the
community—can be logically expressed in a
few words. The public debt is not his debt.
'I he public bankruptcy is not his bank-
ruptcy. The emergency is not of his
making nor of his seeking. If Vic-
toria is disgraced by the failure to
meet her liabilities, the Fat Man is
Victoria for all governmental purposes.
He contrived to be Victoria all through her
period of prosperity, and it is only fair that
he should remain so during her adversity.
When the representatives of Property—the
men who borrowed £50,000,090 on the
nation’s credit, and spent it mainly to tor-
ward their own interests—refuse, as they
have done, to hear a very small measure of
the liability which they themselves created,
and state, in, effect, that the Democracy
must make up the entire deficiency or else
the country must burst, the answer should
be easy. It is, and always has been, the
Fat Mart’s country. If he won’t save his own
country, then the sooner it bursts the better,
« » •
The situation is one where a strong man,
with a strong party behind him, could make
history. ■ Unluckily, most Victorian poli-
ticians are only strong-smelling men —not
strong ones. Premier Turner finds himself
confronted with almost the most remarkable
set of circumstances in Australian history ;
the position is strikingly original—the man,
so far as has yet been seen, is only respect-
able, well-meaning, and a little less common-
place than the average. The emergency,
however, is not new ; it has been visibly
approaching for the last five years or so, and
the fact that the Legislature is taken by
surprise says little for its foresight. The
first beginnings of the trouble descended on
Gillies, in the year when the boom suddenly
began to decay, and he quietly retired from
the scene and handed the difficulty over to
his successor, James Munro. That vener-
able banker found it steadily assuming a
more aggravated form, and presently fled
to London, leaving the spectre of insolvency
to Shiees. Shiels did nothing to meet
the emergency, and it grew steadily worse
under his feeble reign until he handed over
the insoluble problem to Patterson.
Patterson did, if possible, a little less than
Shiels, and the only result of his term of
office was that the public finances, which
had previously been bad, became utterly
desperate. Now' Turner must either find
a remedy, or else retire in his turn and pass
on the ghastly incubus to Deakin, or
Harper, or anyone else who will have it.
The one consistent feature about the in-
cubus is that it gets worse the more it is
passed on, and the country is so tired of the
process that almost anything which could
happen would be a relief.
Federation Geographically Considered*
Blot the Murray system out of the map of Aus-
tralia, and the eastern half of the continent would
be nearly as big a geographical blank as the
western portion. It is the Nile of Australia ; and
few, perhaps, appreciate the extent of its ramifica-
tions, or how its favors are shared among the
principal provinces. Cross the Blue Mountains —
a few hours’ rail from Sydney—and you are on one
of the head waters of this great artery. Go up
to Brisbane and cross the range there, also but a
few, hours’ ride, and you are again on its sources ;
it is the boundary of a long border-line in Victoria;
and South Australia sees its waters safely to the
sea. Putting the area of these four provinces
roughly down at two millions of square miles, the
Murray basin drains 414,253 square miles, op an
eighth pare of the whole continent. Starting from
the northern heads, Queensland claims 101,525 s. m,
of this drainage area, N.S. Wales 234,3(32
Victoria 50,979 s.m. and S. Australia 24,387 s.m.
The bond of union, then, between the four may
bo said to be based upon the one great; rivtyc-
system. Westralia is as aloof from the other
provinces geographically as she is in the spirit
of Federation. At present, with the bumptious-
ness of a hobbledehoy just entering manhood,
tjhe mulga and saltwater province affects to sneer
at Federation. When, however, the rainbow gleam
of the present transient gold-rush has passed, and
the “ dull, sickening thud 11 of the bottom dropping
ou,t of the boom brings the swelling down, Wea-
tralia will probably think differently. Mean-
while, her presence in or out of the coining Federa-
tion is immaterial. Nay, it might even have been
better for Australia, climatically considered, if
that vast, unwieldy tract of aridity had never been
upheaved from the sea (or only in the form of some
islands), and the waves of the Indian Ocean lapped
a shore-line situated somewhere about the present
western boundary of South Australia ; “ but in a
great job like the making of the earth, small
points are apt to bo mislippened.”
# * *
To return to the rivers of the eastern table-land.
In addition to the Murray, Queensland and South
Australia own a joint interest in the big eastern
rivers of the Lake Eyre system. The streams
that unite to form Cooper’s Creek are in Queens-
land. The Diamantina and Eyre’s Qreek are
in the main in that province, and the tropical
rainfall that floods these rivers often brings relief
to the drought-parched regions of South Aus-
tralia. Given some time during the next century
when a system of locking will have widely ex-
tended the navigability of the Murray and
Darlfng, and a like system, combined with the
retention of flood-waters —quite feasible in the
flat interior—has made permanent water - ways
of the channels of Cooper’s Creek and the
Diamantina, the necessity of Federation is at
once apparent. The American who said that Aus-
tralia was “nothing but an infernal ant-bed ”
was right enough, only he did not put it nicely.
If he had said that from end to end one found a
surface configuration which induced a similarity
of pursuits, occupations and amusements amongst
the inhabitants and a little similarity of animals,
birds, insects (ants included), and vegetation, he
would have been more explicit, if less epigram-
matic, That nature has appointed no geographical
divisions is evident in the distribution of the
aborigines. Though some tribes living under
more favorable conditions than others have at-
tained a slightly higher grade physically and men-
tally, the race is essentially the sanifc. From the
Murray flats to the jungles of Cape Writ Penin-
sula, and from the sand-dunes of Fraser’s Island
to the mulga thickets of the Murchison, the
blackfellow and his national weapon, the
boomerang, are unchanged. One can under-
stand, for instance, why Spain should be a distinct
and different country from France, from the
mere result of the geographical structure of the
two; but one cannot understand why a man
living on the Warreuo River should hold his
neighbour, who lives 20 miles lower down, as an
alien and a foreigner. But that is sure to be the
outcome of our growing up independent states
with nothing better than aminvisible line of lati-
tude or longitude to divide one from the other.
# # *
Not only are we jointly interested in the streams
of the upper world, but we are all partners in
those of the under world. Take that much-vexed
and not yet settled question of the permanency of
the artesian supply. Say two provinces, Queens-
land and N.S. Wales, hold different views on the
question. N.S. Wales believes in the probable
falling off of the output and institutes a strict
system of supervision and economy. On the other
hand, Queen dand considers the supply inex-
haustible, and consequently takes no steps.
Water drawn from the same deep-buried reservoir
may be being rigidly saved on one side of the •
boundary and lavishly wasted on the other..
Again, to show how absurdly our artificial boun-
daries work, take the case of the Georgina River,,
in Queensland. It heads in that province, flows
across the boundary into the Northern Territory,,
takes a bend, and presently returns to Queens-
land. There is not a particle of difference in the ■
value of the country on its banks, but the lessee -
of pastoral land in Queensland has paid a much -
higher rental than his brother squatter across the •
border. In this case the difference is accentuated;!
by the river passing to and fro between the two •
provinces, but it is the same throughout the con-
tinent. Between N.S. Wales and Victoria the
Murray forms a natural boundary, and between;-
N.S. Wales and Queensland there is a fainti'.
attempt at one, but otherwise we are like four
people living in a large room drawing invisible •
lines on the floor and each warning the other to ■
keep within his bounds.
# * *
But there is still a wider view of the -
question. The territory we have to deal with -
may be said to have to have been handed over, to .
us, in the rough, to finish. Naturally, we have
first settled down on the most finished parts and! I
left the remainder for the aftercomers, yeti this -
remainder has, we now find, a value of its.own.
But while its reclamation or development could: be ■
well accomplished by united action, our waste-
territory will never become productive in the -
present state of things. Under Federation every .-
dry creek and parched billabong should be filled I
either with the impounded flood or artesian i
water, and thousands of miles of productive •
territory added to our possessions. The •
proper natural features of the country would 1
be followed, quite irrespective of artificial. I
boundary lines, and Nature aided in the •
work she is still striving to accomplish. Al-'
though it is customary to speak of desert country ,
as occupying a large share of the interior of the ■
continent, experience and history both teach us
that “deserts,” in tropical and semi-tropical I
climes, cease to exist when once they are assailed !
by the resources of money or numbers. The Aus- ■
tralian desert is a desert truly in its primitive .
state, a desert in its loneliness and lifelessness,,
a desert in the harsh and stunted nature of its:
vegetation, a desert in its grim monotony and I
repellent front, its aridity and desolation, but lett
the magic elixir of water be applied and all these-
attributes disappear. The hot, dry climate be-
comes a fostering mother of life, to be depended!
on for its constant uniformity ; the hard, prickly,
growth, spawn of the hot wind and sun,
pears ; and the aimless creeks, but half-formed by
the rare and doubtful rainful, once filled with tins
subterranean water, become long serpentine lagoons
—centres of life and moisture.
* • * /
All this may seem visionary and Utopian, but
it is within the power of,.a Federation to accom-
plish. So-far, there has been no jiheck to the west-
ward march of the drill, and there seems no reason
why there should be. Experiments have now
finally proved that the old idea that artesian water
is useless for irrigation purposes may go the way
of many other old ideas. In the hands of a Fede-
rated Australia the reclamation of Central and
even Western-Central Australia is possible, and
Nature has* even prepared the way for it in the
adaptability of the soil and the formation of the
surface. The unification of the four provinces,
-Whose life-blood may be said to be the Murray
and its host of tributaries, must eventually lead
to the reclamation of the whole continent for
purposes of close settlement. What could not be
attempted by one province single-handed would
be within the reach of them all united, and
the work would be no /longer provincial but
national. Our long seaboard is another
item which Nature has lib.-rally endowed
us with as a whole, although, as our arti-
ficial boundaries stand, it is unequally dis-
tributed. With all interprovincial tariffs swept
away, the good natural ports would be as available
for one province as foranother, and thefruitlesslabor
and expense of trying to m dee artificial harbors
would be doneaway with. The longer Federation is
delayed, the greater become the difficulties in the
way, for every year adds to the exclusive feeling
of each province. Trie N.S. Welshman becomes
more New South Welsh and the Queenslander
more Queensland i h ; while all the time there is
ilttle difference, geographical or climatic, between
the. districts in which they reside. There are
about five centuries of work to settle Australia
throughout and make it habitable even to the
most barren tracts, and when once we realise
that we are the people of a continent, not of a
province only, we shall probably commence to
think about starting on the job. With a smaller
rainfall and more meagre appliances the
Romans pushed their way far into the Sahara .
and made fruitful spots there, commencing
what the French are now going on with. The
Assyrians did the same, and we have evidences of
great works of reclamation done by nations whose
very names are lost. /With all the new science of
the world within our grasp, and the hereditary
pioneer spirit in our hearts, the foundation of a
great nation that will people Australia from end
to end should be now laid in the eastern half and,
by the natural and irresistible progress of expan-
sion, gradually embrace the continent.
ERNEST FAVENG.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Fat Man's Version of the Vic. Crisis.
There is something especially beautiful about'
the unanimity of the capitalistic press on one
aspect of the recent political crisis in Victoria.
The point on which it is so unanimous is that
the Turner Government was defeated because
it proposed to reduce the salaries of M.’sP. as


part of the general scheme of retrenchment.
On the strength of this assertion almost every
Fat Man paper in Australia has rushed to the
front with a leading article, showing how the
paid politician—and especially the paid labor
politician —is prepared to sacrifice everybody
save himself to the necessity for retrenchment
—how he cheerfully sacked hundreds of civil
servants, and retrenched the salaries ot the
remainder, and cut down sick pay, and econo-
mised on Judges, telegraph-messengers, school-
teachers, and Ministers of the Grown, with
equal readiness, but preferred to overset the
Ministry, and waste all the work of the
;session rather than have his own salary re-
duced below £270 per annum, ihe inference
drawn from this state of things is that the
politician—and especially the paid labor poli-
tician—is a grasping, cold-blooded person who
dives for his salary only, and has no vestige o
I patriotism in his composition. 1 his, tor
instance, is Sydney Telegraph s reading of the
11 'litrf who, before the constituencies and in the House
had strenuously supported the Ministry and backed them
up in all thfir measures of a progressive character, sud-
denly discovered that the moment that their pockets were
touched political allegiance to their leader was no louder
possible. The result is the humi hatma sp 'Ctaole of a
Ministry bein<c practically defeated by a selhsli, salary
grabbing clique, by whom political principle was shame-
lessly sacrificed for the sake of pecuniary and peisonal
The S.M. Herald takes the same view, if pos-
sible a little more enthusiastically
The majority of the Victorian Assembly-in this re-
sembling very closely majorities of other Assemblies
■which it is not now necessary to name—were deeply
shocked and grieved to see so commercial a spirit dis-
played by the Ministry. But they never for a moment
hesitated as to what they ought to do, and in resolving to
.do it even though the path of duty should he over a
shattered Ministry and over the ruins of their country.
When they spoke so freely and so imprudently about
cutting down their salaries, the tacit reservation was, of
course, implied that what was meant was the salaries of
everybody else, from the Governor to the poorly-paid
office messenger. But the moment a rash Ministry, rely-
ing on the explicit declarations of members themselves
that they would consider no retrenchment complete that
did not include cutting down their own salaries pro-
ceeded in all simplicity of mind to give effect to these
pledges, it is promptly determined that, whatever may be
the cost or peril to the country at this juncture, that
Ministry has got to go.
These two extracts are fair specimens of the
attitude of the Fat Man press in general. It
assumed this attitude all over Australasia on
the morning after the trouble arose; there
was no collusion about the matter in
fact, there was no time for collusion ; an
unerring Fat Man instinct led all the capi-
talistic press in the same direction at the
same instant. And yet the whole story is
one great, unvarnished lie, and the fact that all
the organs of capital should have risen up
together and simultaneously told the same lie
is, therefore, all the more remarkable. The
real narrative, as told in Hansard, is as unlike
the Fat Man paper’s version as can well be con-
ceived. Years ago the Victorian politician en-
joyed the very moderate wage of £3OO per
annum, but when bad times came and an all-
round reduction of 10 per cent, was made in
official salaries, the Assembly cheerfully re-
duced its own salary on the same scale
as the rest, making it £270. In view of the
continued bad times and the necessity for still
more economy, Premier Turner proposed—
and the Fat Man, who loathed payment in any
;shape, cheered him on—to make a further re-
duction to £2OO, and, after a long discussion,
■carried on mainly by half-a-dozen members, the
second reading of the Bill was carried by 60
wotes against 20—about the biggest and most
.enthusiastic majority that the Turner Govern-
ment has had on any question. An attempt
was made in committee by J. B. Patterson
to make the payment £250 instead of £2OO, but
Turner’s majority still stood by him, and the
meat-man of Castlemaine was suppressed by
another substantial majority. Then, the im-
portant business being over, most of the mem-
bers went home to bed, and the remnant, being
very tired, proposed, at a late hour, to adjourn
and do likewise, and carried the adjournment
motion against the Govt, by eight votes in a
very thin House ; whereupon Premier Turner,
whose nervous system was apparently in a bad
state, made a foolish exhibition of himself by
threatening immediate resignation. That was
all the crisis there was. It had absolutely no
more connection with the Payment of Members
Bill than it had with the Japanese war or the
recent snowstorm in Hungary. There was no
question of patriotism or want of patriotism in
the matter ; the beginning and middle and
end of it was that the Assembly was worn out,
and was still suffering from the Christmas fes-
tivities, and badly wanted to get to bed. The
ingenuity of the Fat Man press, in making so
much out of so little, was a clumsy lie from first
to last. No one but a Minister in a bad state
of liver and exasperation would have regarded
so trivial an affair as a defeat at all, and if
Turner had been his usual self the incident
would have passed quite unnoticed. Not being
I his usual self he blew up in a vaguely irres-
ponsible fashion to the astonishment of his
party and of the House generally.
Turner “Defines his Position.”
When Premier Turner lost his head after Vic.
Assembly had carried a one-horse motion of
adjournment against him, and blew up like a
Chinese cracker, he started out in his excite-
ment to define the position of the Government,
and did it a lot more truthfully than he would
have done in his calmer moments. “So far as
this Government is concerned,” said Turner,
“ it is not going to remain in office to be dic-
tated to by members.” Under these circum-
stances the advantage of having members at all
is not very apparent. According to a legislative
fiction, M.’sP. are supposed to rule the country,
and the policy of the Government is supposed
to represent the aggregate wisdom or foolish-
ness of the Parliamentary majority. In reality,
under the blessed system of Government by
Contradiction, when the Government goes
wrong, according to the opinion of the majority,
and the majority tries to make it go right, then
it is its duty to resign and give place to
another Cabinet which will also resign as
soon as it is asked to run the public
business as Parliament thinks it ought
to do. The duty of every high - minded
Ministry, under the present system, is to resist
the dictation of the Assembly and “get
up on its ear” whenever that body
attempts to assert itself. Under these
mixed conditions, The Bulletin would respect-
fully ask Mr. Turner what he thinks Parlia-
ment is intended for ? If it is not to dictate to
the Ministry then it is evident that, in Mr.
Turner’s opinion, the Ministry should do “ what-
ever it darned well likes,” and the business
of the Assembly is merely to vote for whatever
the Ministry proposes. Under these conditions
the country doesn’t want an Assembly. All it
wants is to elect a Cabinet once every three
years or so, and let it whoop along just as it
pleases till its term expires ; and when its term
expires the business of the country is to re-elect
it and let it whoop along again. On second
thoughts, however, The Bulletin doesn’t see
what the country has to do with the matter
either. If the country has no right to dictate
to the Ministry indirectly, through its elected
representatives, then it has no right to dictate
to it directly or any other way. And if it has
no right to dictate what the Ministry
shall do, it becomes an open question if
it has any right to dictate what the
Ministry shall be. When the system of Party
Government, as Turner understands it, reaches
its full development, the Cabinet will probably
hold office for life and then hand down its billet
to its son, or its nephew, or its wife’s eldest
son by a former marriage, or something like
that. The situation resolves itself into two
alternatives —either the country is to dictate to
the Ministry or it is not. Turner says it is
not, therefore the only logical result is a here-
ditary Cabinet which won’t even pretend to be
responsible to any one.
Railway Deadheads.
Since the publication of the recent stupendous
list of New South Wales railway deadheads, a
good many papers have arisen to point out, for
the several thousandth time, that it is the duty
of Commissioner Eddy to stop this old-
established robbery, and that, in not stopping
it, he has failed in his duty. This sort of
experience goes a long way to account for the
Commissioner’s anxiety to leave New South
Wales for good. He was engaged by Parlia-
ment for the purpose of making the railways
pay, of running them on commercial principles
and putting an end to abuses. Parliament
demands that he should put down the dead-
head evil as one of the abuses, and harries him,
at the same time, for free passes for its wife
and all its relatives. If he doesn’t grant them he
makes a bitter enemy of every begging M.L.A,
whose application for a free-pass has been re-
jected, and that individual and his kind accuse
him in the House of mismanagement and
nepotism, and sool their newspapers on him,
and compel him to defend himself at great
expense, before Parliamentary commissions,
against all sorts of charges, and refuse to defray
his heavy legal expenses afterwards. If he
does grant them, someone rises up after a while
to ask for a return of railway deadheads, and
when the appalling list comes to hand, the
press and the members who got the free passes
want to know why Commissioner Eddy doesn’t
do his duty and abolish this great swindle upon
the public exchequer. Yet no one among all
these bogus reformers proposes to relieve the
Commissioner of the responsibility of acting as
sole buffer between his greedy employers
and the public revenue. Not one indivi-
dual who demands passes from Eddy, and
afterwards curses him for granting them,
and would curse him still harder if he didn’t
grant them, even suggests that the effective
way out of the difficulty is to abolish free-
passes altogether, except in the case of railway
employes travelling on duty only. That is the
simple and obvious remedy, but nobody wants
that kind of remedy. Then if the Government
wanted to help along any distinguished visitor,
the only way to do it would be for the Treasurer
to hand the distinguished one the price of his
or her ticket, and explain the matter afterwards
the best way he could in his Financial State-
ment, and then Parliament could order that the
amount thus expended be deducted from his
salary. Will some democratic M.L.A. try the
experiment of bringing in a bill to this effect ?
A Specious Proposal:
The Victorian Property party raises its flat-
catching cry for Parliamentary Reform at an
opportune moment. Retrenchment is in the
air, the character of the Legislative Assembly
—the character of the precious Council is not
touched upon in this “reform” question—
stands only a little higher than it stood before
last elections, and Fat Man’s agitation for
fewer members at a very much reduced salary
receives honest support from democrats who
haven’t thought the matter out. At a first
glance every Senate in Australia appears
too bulky, and, therefore, too expensive.
Payment of members is absolutely necessary
to a fair representation of the people, and
.£3OO a year is, in The Bulletin’s opinion,
the smallest payment consistent with public
faith in the principle. If the unwealthy section
of Vic. Assembly agrees to a reduction of
salary out of respect for the spirit of the times
it doesn’t follow that the aforesaid principle
has suffered a fall in value because the
Treasurer’s deficit is steadily rising. Nor will
any wise democrat give ear to the Tory hubbub
which is certain to come anon, when the
Labor members shall feel justified in demanding
a return to the normal .£3OO rate. Common-
sense may be bamboozled, just now, by the
party that would gladly supply the country
with a small Assembly free of charge. But the
folly of allowing the thin end of Eat Man’s
wedge to touch the Act that enables thin men
to have a hand in law manufacture will soon be
understood by the very voters who created
the danger. It is easy to see the mistake—
when pointed out—in reducing the payment of
members. The error of advocating a reduction
in their numbers is rather less obvious to the
average eye, which, after all, is a very dull
optic, with a wonderful capacity for holding dust.
The fact that little Great Britain gets along with
one honorary M.P. to every (50,000 inhabitants
or thereabouts, is hastily accepted as evidence
that Victoria carries five times as many paid
legislators as the country requires. “ What
do we want with 95 members ?” is a common
enquiry. The common reply is, “ Heaven
knows!” followed by a sage shaking of the
head. The Lord knows why His servant Fat
Man encourages the idea that a smaller House
is desirable. Fewer members would mean an
increase in the area of electoral divisions, and
the bigger the electorate the smaller a poor
man’s chance of heading a wealthy candidate at
the poll. Remove 35 seats, as proposed by the
Voice of Proputty, from the Victorian As-
sembly, and, so far from having a brisker,
brighter competition for the remaining 60
places, the country would find itself represented
more and more by leisured capitalists, able to
spend the money and time necessary to a care-
ful “working” of large, sparsely-populated
districts. That is the Parliamentary Reform
desired by the land-owning and Freetrade class,
and now advocated by their press organ. Fat
Man’s scheme is like a cheap, sheeny dress
material in Sargood’s store. It appears good
goods until one holds it up to the light, and
sees the figure of Sargood through its thin
texture.
Trial by Cabinet Minister.
A simple means of increasing the Victorian
revenue without the aid of a new bill will
doubtless be adopted by the Customs Depart-
ment sooner or later. At present, the influen-
tial importer whose goods are invoiced at less
than cost price, is encouraged in his fraud by
the Minister’s indisposition to prosecute when,
perchance, a particularly glaring case came
before him. These swindles are compounded
in secret. The big importer pays a fine to the
department, which fine is not calculated to
make him honest in the future, and presently a
Chinaman is proceeded against on a much smaller
charge, financially speaking, in order that the
majesty of the Law may be asserted. A very
poor excuse for drawing this distinction between
BuadiNS & Co., of Flinders Lane, and Wing
Cow, of Little Bourke-street, is offered by the
Customs Dept. It is suggested that Buggins
and his commercial friends are such deter-
mined liars and artful dodgers that the depart-
ment would generally fail to prove its case,
so the private compromise is therefore a
prudent expedient. Only a political mind
could deceive itself with such an argument
as this. A fraudulent merchant must be fairly
cornered before he will pay a fine, and the evi-
dence which he can’t wriggle away from at the
Customs would be even more overpowering in a
law-court. Moreover, money paid in private
to avoid a prosecution that would break down
is money illegally obtained. The Vic. Minister
of Customs, on his own showing, extorts cash to
which he is not entitled. Furthermore, he cheats
many a hungry lawyer out of the job of suc-
cessfully defending a trader whose guilt, if an
accomplished fact, can’t be proved. Here is a
potent reason why the Legislative Assembly,
where every ninth man is licensed to wear a
wig, should insist upon a fair trial of all alleged
revenue - robbers. The system of trial by
Cabinet Minister is bad all round. Bad for the
Minister’s moral sense, bad for the suppositious
victim who allows himself to be bounced, bad
for legal business, exceedingly bad for the
public pocket. The effect of a strictly stern
and honest administration of the Customs
Dept, would be to raise the average invoice-
price of dutiable goods at once and for ever.
Firms that swindle now because swindling is a
good spec., and their precious characters are
not injured thereby, can be made honest by the
fear of exposure, whilst the inveterate rogues
who can’t trade straight, not having been built
that way, are bound to draw their frauds
milder when they have to run the risk of day-
light prosecutions.
The fatheaded London shopkeeper who con-
siders the Queen a good thing for trade is not
alone in his ignorance. He has supporters in
the Vic. Legislative Assembly, for o ie place.
Harper, the pious grocery dealer, and Foster
Rogers, an eccentric Tory with a socialistic
kink in his brain, were both of them against a
reduction in the Governor’s salary, on the
ground that it would be bad for trade. The
difference between £lO,OOO and £5OOO a year
would mean a loss of .£5OOO to “ legitimate
circulation ” in Victoria, said these two poli-
tical economists. By the same token, of course,
an addition of tyvo millions to Hopetoun’s
annual stipend would improve the legitimate
circulation by that amount, provided that he
spent it like a noble earl. In fact, the country
couldn’t do better than give all its loose
cash to his Excellency as often as it
had any loose cash, and ask him to do
some more circulating. The world suffers
considerably from shopkeepers’ theories.
Loyalty to the Crown is very much a matter of
believing that a Court ball “ causes money to
be spent which wouldn’t be spent otherwise.”
The House of Peers is a benefit to humanity in
the eyes of Harper and Rogers, seeing that it
circulates a lot of money in keeping up its
“dignity.” The late Lord blackguard Ailes-
bury was a champion useful peer because he
circulated so far above the average. Trade is
deeply indebted to the Jubilee Juggins. An
uncle, or somebody, bequeathed a fortune to
the young man, and he sacrificed it, like a true
patriot, on the altar of commerce. Cod
bless him for that ! He didn’t work and
produce wealth in any form, but he circulated
coin, therefore it would have been a thousand
pities to poll-axe the Juggins and hand his
estate over to the Chancellor of the Exchequer.
Believe all this, and you will be saved—or
rather, you will cease to be a confounded
democrat, which amounts to the same thing.
The shopkeeper’s dream of a universal aris-
tocracy, with an unlimited circulating power,
doesn’t quite harmonise with Fat Man’s extra
vote for Thrift—but no matter. The one sad
circumstance we are considering at present is
the loss of .£5OOO to Victoria’s circulation all
along of reducing the vice-regal salary.
Messrs. Hogue and O’Sullivan, M.’sP., joined
hands the other day in an appeal to N.S.VV.
Minister Young on behalf of the unemployed.
They assured the Minister that the unemployed
were in a parlous state and should be provided
with work, but as all eould not be put into
harness, they urged the immediate employment
of some 25 or so, whom they specially repre
sented. Hogue and O’Sullivan should sit
down and ask themselves why they were created
and sent into the political world. Was ranker
political patronage than this ever aimed at ?
“ These twenty-five men,” they said, “ are not
agitators or Domain spouters, but good, ca-
pable and trustworthy workingmen.” So far as
Editor Hogue is concerned, it may be men-
tioned that the difference is little between a
man who agitates with his pen and one who
agitates with his tongue. The journalist is
always on the alert for something to excite
interest, and if some people prefer talking to
writing the preference is not to be charged to
their detriment. It is pretty much the same
impulse, the same character of mind, and the
same conception of duty which drive one man
to write an article and another to jump on a
stump and talk. As regards Mr. O’Sullivan’s
fine scorn for “agitators,” it was as
an agitator that Mr. O’Sullivan started,
and, until his salary and fat perquisites
bulged out his pocketbook, as an agitator he
lived, and no doubt, if lean days come again, as
an agitator he will die. Which is not the
slightest harm, provided he agitate for what is
right. The heart of the vicious position both
men took up beats in the innuendo that
agitators are necessarily not good working men.
This has been the convenient phrase of all
tyrannies, of all despotisms, of all superiorities
founded on pretence instead of desert, and has
been applied to newspapers as well as to men
for ages.
The usual weekly “ tragedy ” has occurred at
Forbes (N.S.W.). Another zealous and intel-
ligent policeman has shot his man—a diminu-
tive Italian resident of the township, known
as “ Little Bennie.” The policeman was asked
to sleep in a house whose inmates complained
of night annoyances. According to his own
story, he was roused about one o’clock by a
noise on the verandah. It was very dark, but
he looked out and saw three men, one of whom
raised the open window-sash slightly. The
constable rose, jumped out of the window,
revolver in hand, fired in the air, and called on
the men to stand. Two of them ran away
and have never been heard of since. The
third was a miserable little cranky Italian
whom the constable chased into a cul-
de-sac. The Italian tried to dodge and get
out, and the constable shot him through the
head and killed him. This is the constable’s
own story; and the dead man can’t deny it.
The Italian was unarmed, but a screw driver
was found in his pocket by another constable,
and a pair of slippers—only one pair of slippers
—were at the gate of the house. The man was
possibly an intending burglar, but, burglar or
none, the law declares that a policeman shall
make every effort to arrest his quarry before
taking life. In this case the constable
appears to have made no effort whatever. He
was in no bodily danger ; the Italian was a
resident well-known to him ; the Italian was
much the smaller and weaker man ; yet the
constable appears to have lost his head and
blazed away at random. The other two men
may have been real or they may have been mere
men of buckram —myths of the disordered con-
stabulary imagination. And the usual wooden-
headed jury sitting on the body the same day,
a dozen hours or so after the slaughter, brings
in a verdict of justifiable homicide, and thanks
the constable for his valour! The case urgently
demands enquiry.

correspondent that in according free passes over

Smith (2),” and “ Lady Joskins and daughters
(6),” managed to travel to Australia “on the
nod” per (say) the Messageries Maritimes and

Supply and Demand.
The Argus, dealing with the Newcastle (N.S.W.) col-
liers’ troubles, sheds a few green tears over “ the hapless
men who have to suffer for a terrible act of folly com-
mitted by past leaders,” holding that “ the difficulty is
largely due to the great strikes of several years ago,”
but owners are sooner or later compelled by the pressure
of competition to reduce rates to the level of poorer col-
lieries which first get the advantage of the excess of
labour, and there vs no power on earth that can prevent
them.’’ — Bulletin italics.
Lost the cause for which you’re fighting, futile
all your hopes and vain,
For the Loud has set His face, and marked a
way,
And no power on earth can turn it, or can plan
a course again,
Whilst men hunger, and they thirst, and toil,
and pray.
Like the sun, the Law is steadfast, none may
change it, none command —
Man is but the servile creature of supply and of
demand !
Gnarled and bent and stained with labour,
sweating, striving ’neath the goad,
Look not onward, look not upward for a
star.
Do and dree in brutish patience, set your
shoulders to the load,
For ye can but be the wage-slaves that ye
are.
In His image God made men, and gave to them
the teeming earth,
There to fight for life in fury from their luck-
less hour of birth.
And the few who have not fallen ’neath the
curse of Adam’s flaw
Mock your weariness with false and empty
sighs,
And with mouthing of the mighty and uncon-
querable Law
Made of fraud, and force, and ignorance, and
lies.
So the rich will spread the Gospel, while its
sentiments imply
Theirs the pleasure to demand, and yours the
duty to supply.
Melb.
E. DYSON.

mentary return, on the subject of New South
Wales State-railway dead-heads, reveals some
interesting facts, but the root-facts of the case

pockets ? The inquiry should be pursued
