Mr Dear Moorabinda—
I must say that I quite fail to see why they re-
produced those lists of women dead-heads on the
railways in a Sydney daily recently, although, of
course, one must take one’s newspaper sensations
thankfully, asking no questions, about this time of
year. But I don’t quite appreciate the horrible
spectacle in printer’s ink supposed to be afforded
by these aforementioned lists, although, as I have
never travelled “ on the nod” in all my born days,
1 ought to be thunder-stricken at the bare idea of
anybody else doing so, undoubtedly. The
overwhelming majority of these unprofitable
passengeresses appear as “wives of M.’sP.”
Well, I honestly think that the more free
passes the average member can beg, borrow or
steal for his wife (always making it distinctly
understood that he mustn’t go up the line with a
tall, thin, dark wife, and down with a short, fat
helpmate with Jersey hair), the higher will be the
moral tone observed in ten-minute tunnels. Also,
there will be noticeable decrease in rash hopes
born only to wither on the stem in unsophisti-
cated rustic bosoms which mistook the gentle-
man in the long hat for a respectable single man.
Nor_ do I want to throw any violent
aspersions on the dead-head duchesses, “stiff”
honorable Miss So-and-So’s, and free-listed Lady
Toplofties. It seems strange, perhaps, that
these people, whose prototypes scatter diamonds
on their hirelings in the pages of fiction, cannot in
real life pay for their railway-tickets; but while
Australia is a continent dotted with Gov’ment-
houses these abuses we will always have with us.
Where is the portfolio’d politician who would
dare to hang “Free list entirely suspended ” over
the door of his department, and brutally tell a
Governor’s mother-in-law applying for a sleeping-
berth on the nod that Her Grace, if she wished to
travel cheaply, would have to go as a corpse?
Wtiile Australia tries to behave itself as a little
tin-pot imitation of England, with Governors,
seconded by titled Premiers, to lead the way, our
taxpayers should decidedly take all these things
as part of the expenses consequent upon maintain-
ing a fashionable establishment. It’s all very
How often do Wo not wish during our hot
summer days to have something to refresh us
with ; and how near it is to hand. Just give the
sanitary toilet requisite “Amykos” a trial, and
you will never be without same as a refreshing,
cooling and wholesome compound to pour into
the washing-basin ; or, used as a mouth-wash and
gargle, you will always keep your mouth in a
healthy, clean and purified condition. To
be had from all chemists and perfumers at Is. 6d.
f>er bottle. Wholesale representative for Austra-
asia, C. A. Falstedt, Sydney.*
well to groan over statistics that don’t talk
back, but how does the thing look in
reality? For example, Lady Jersey’s one
and only chum—Lady Galloway—figures on
the black-list. But what a social bungler a
Minister would seem if he refused a Governor a
free pass for his wife’s bosom friend ! It’s an old
story that no man can serve two masters. If a
man is a Gov’menthouse politician he is necessarily
easy game for the stream of distinguished visitors,
who go through him for all his office is worth,
and leave him to face the Opposition benches when
the band begins to play. And, if he isn’t a
Gov’menthouse legislator, who is he? So long as
there is a Governor left on these shores so long is
there bound to be an everlasting golden political
goose on the parliamentary roost for the Luke
and Duchess of Plaza-Toro to exploit at their own
sweet will. And that’s my ultimatum.
Tailsing of M’s.P., a lengthy and ennobbled ex-
Premier would still seem to fill the public eye and
be a dress-circle hero, in spite of being somewhat
out of the running. It seems a lady wrote him a
most furious love-letter the other day. Not that
M’s.P. think much of a little thing like that; but
this billet-doux was written in the lady’s gore,
drawn from her romantic veins, first pierced by a
silver stiletto with which she had killed a man in
Napoli. That was her little tale, at any rate,
though I suspect she got the whole effect out of a
steel nib and a bottle of red ink.
The story goes that the moderately-brand-new
bride of a Maoriland mail-boat captain was not
taken seriously on a recent trip, owing to her
badly-concealed idea that the wife of a steamboat
authority is a sort of queen of the deep so long as
she sails under the flag of her husband’s company.
From the very first hoisting of the gangway the
lady had been tentatively doing the honors of the
ship, though it wasn’t her husband’s boat—a far-
seeing management doesn’t allow Captain and
Mrs. Binnacle to travel together, “ as there would
then be two bosses aboard instead of one”—but
it was not until they were well into the voyage
that Mrs. Brassbuttons began to be really amusing.
Stopping too long on shore at, say, Rio, she sent
an imperative note to the captain—” Stop the boat,
I shall be late.” In spite of this, when she arrived
at the wharf there was the liner in sea-going trim
slowly steaming away ! By the help of a fleet
boat and frantic signallings with her gamp, she
managed to reach the ship and was hauled on
board anyhow, choking with indignation. “ Didn’t
you receiye my note?” she acridly demanded of
the captain, as if the failure of the missive to hit
the bull’s-eye- was the only explanation possible.
tl Oh, yes,” was the careless reply, ‘‘but surely
you understand that we never wait for ordinary
passengers.” They say that the bride wore a
more deferential air for the rest of the run. The
big mailboat companies are wise in their genera-
tion. There really can’t be two bosses on a ship.
The farewell banquet and reception given re-
spectively to Vice-Admiral Bowden-Smith and
the Admiraless by the citizens of Sydney in the
Centennial Hall the other evening, were pleasant
festivities and much less perfunctory than such
affairs usually seem. As a rule, when people band
together to give a distinguished personage a meal,
it generally means that half-a-dozen enthusiasts,
grateful for past hospitalities, have been clever
enough to hoodwink a number of uninterested
people into booming what ought to have been a
club dinner into a public banquet. But the ban-
3uet under notice was surprisingly free from the
isaffeeted person who shows symptoms of a desire
to call out “No !” and “ Turn him out!” when the
chairman starts upon the oily task of flattering
the guest of the evening.
It was rather mischievous of the departing
Admiral to be so enthusiastic over the naval
dwelling at Kirribilli Point which he alluded to as
a “ pleasant residence which it probably is
when the wind is in the east and flowing towards
the hide-depot next door. If it was his purpose
to lure the new Admiral into slinging his hammock
there he appears to have succeeded. Rear-Admiral
Bridge has expressed his intention of haunting
-Admiralty House just as much as his predecessor
did, though it is hard to see what good that will
do. A grass-widower like Admiral B. might just
as well hang out at the top of his own mizzen-
mast for all the social use he is likely to be from
a feminine point of view.
I thought the departing Admiral threw a new
light on naval suffering in the beastly kawlinies
when he told the anecdote of the brother officer
who ‘‘took umbrage” when a Tasmanian once
pointed an impressive finger at H.M.S. Katoomba
and said “ That’s one of our ships.” The officer
in question bitterly resented the possessive pro-
noun, first person plural—when applied to the
subsidised man-o’-war. Considering that Aus-
tralia contributes the whole cost of the
Auxiliary Squadron, and pays, that iden-
tical officer’s salary, I don’t quite see why any
tax-payer shouldn’t allude to the ships as “ours,”
if he likes. The only way out of the difficulty ’is
for the incensed naval Johnnie aforesaid to leave
off talking about “my ship” and always allude to,
the Auxiliary fleet as “ those beastly kawlonial
punts, don'tcherknow.” But it is vain to imagine
such an effort. Deprive a Britisher of his pet
possessive pronoun (“my lawyer,” “ mv hotel ”
“my banker”) and he will wither like an un-
watered flower.
The new Admiral spoke at the banquet—but I
got the best view of him at the reception. Admiral
Bridge is an abbreviated hero with a thin, rather
pale face, trimmed with- a scrubby moustache
beard and whiskers. Owing to his being a rear-
admiral, he doesn’t train so much gold over his
person as we have lately been accustomed to, and
altogether ha impressed me as a marine potentate
who would be scared to death if a deputation of
women ever waited upon him to urge him into
Riving a kettle-drum. His suite, however, looks as
if it would bo equal to a dozen such emergen-
cies. Secretary Hopkins (who now holds the
marine social fate of Potts Point in the hollow of
his hand) is a tall and athletic youth facially
resembling Robert Brough, though, of course, he
considers himself much lovelier. Blag-Lieutenant
Huff (not Gov. Huff’s eldest son, as has been
stated : his Lxcellency’s first-born is in the army)
is also a clean-shaven Adonis of the deepest dye,
and other satellites of the naval system seem to be
more or less up to date.
~ Ik i s touching to see what a back number the old
‘suite ” becomes when the new turns up I Its com-
ponent parts stalk about like skeletons at the feast,
while Society belledom longs to see them hustled on
board the mailboat ere they have time to give the
new men any pointers as to how fond Miss Blank
is of looking at cabin-albums, or what marvellous
uses Miss Hash puts ship-hatbands to. Admiral
Bridge and his festive following have come to stay
three years, by the way. It is a little too early
for Australia to congratulate herself on the subject
just yet awhile.
A fashionable gathering farewelled Mrs. Bowden-
Sraith and her unaffected daughters at the public
reception given in their honor. The great hall
was gay with flags and potted plants, while inces-
sant band and organ music made it necessary for
people to bellow their graceful valedictory remarks
to the heroines of the hour. The Admiraless made
her last public appearance in mauve silk, with
black lines, and carried a charming bouquet of
roses and water-lilies—a get-up in which Mrs.
Bowden Smith looked so well that one found it
hard to believe that she owns to having seen
Charles Kean play Henry V. In a farewell letter
to Mrs. Rignold the Admiraless confessed to
having seen only two Henry Yees, “Charles
Kean, at Hrury Lane, and your husband in
Sydney.” Judging by the latter-day appearance
of the kindly lady, it is evident that Kean was
no stickler about admitting babies in arms.
Miss Bowden Smith wore a becoming yellow
brocade, Miss Mildred Bowden Smith (who made
her debut at a Sydney Govt. House ball) sported
shell pink, while a small sister, in pure white,
her committee bouquet with obvious
pride. There was a fair sprinkling of good-look-
ing gowns on the floor, considering that it is now
the full swing of the recuperating season. The
committee-ladies were all got-up to the last hair
of their heads, and greatly conduced to the success
of the evening. You knew they were committee-
ladies because they each wore a badge like an
attenuated archer’s target over their hearts.
Whether these things were intended to suggest
Cupid’s darts to the new naval contingent is quite
beyond my reckoning.
la] king about the navy, a Melbourne daily
waxed indignant recently over the fact that the
Auxiliary Squadron prefers Barm Cove to Hob-
son s Bay, and tried its best to provoke the Vic-
torian Government into asserting the obvious
rights of Melbourne belledom to a thicker slice of
the gallant tars’ society than they are in the habit
of receiving. And, seeing that Victoria really pays
her share of the “ naval agreement ” that keeps
‘our ships ” in commission, it does seem a hard
thing that the Southern province hasn’t even one
society engagement to show for it.
I think the surest remedy is to run two Mel-
bourne Cups instead of one. A stern sense of
duty always takes the whole fleet —from the flag-
ship down to the fussiest little torpedo-boat—over
to Hobson’s Bay towards the end of October to
guard Port Phillip from the foe, and supposing
another Cup were run in March, or thereabouts, a
half-yearly naval hegira would undoubtedly take
place.
It really does seem a shame that the Melbourne
maiden hasn’t an equal chance with her Sydney
sister in trying to bring Captain Corcoran to the
altar—and, yet, despite the Squadron’s apparent
predilection for Barm Cove, Toorak hasn’t done so
badly from a naval point of view. The only Aus-
tralian Admiraless in the world is a Victorienne.
I hear that a gilded Maoriland youth fled to
Australia the other day under painful circum-
stances. He wrote to a lady proposing marriage,
and then for a whole week he. waited in vain for
an answer. Being driven to fury at last by the
lady’s cold neglect, he arose and proposed to
another girl and was accepted. Next day the
original girl, who had been out of town, returned
and received the letter, and replied with an enthu-
siastic affirmative; and the distracted vouth,
finding himself affianced to two girls at once, des-
pairingly packed up his high collar and left hia
native shores.
A sister of Lady Carington is about to. share the
Luck of Lden Hall,” as she is shortly to be
married to young Musgrave, a Cumberland
baronet of much wealth, who, if I am not mis-
taken, was in Australia not long ago in the
leading-strings of a Mr. Wyndham Tuffnel. He, as
the proprietor of the romantic Eden Hall, owns
the celebrated jdass vase, which tradition says was
stolen from fairies, and which brings such good
fortune to its possessor that it is called the “ Luck
of E len Hall.” Bearful pains and penalties are
attached to its loss or destruction, and therefore
it is diligently cared for, and is only used on very
grand occasions. Miss Nora Harbord is the
lady s name, and she counts three more years than
her fiance, who came of age only a short time
ago.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
Melbourne Confidences.
Melbourne, January 15.
The Oamperdown chemist, called upon in the
doctor’s absence for professional assistance to Lady
Hopetoun when she was bitten by a dog, is
evidently another of Fortune’s minions. He is said
to have met the emergency nobly, with calm, un-
moved front and a composite treatment, including
all known remedies for bite, sting, scratch, wound,
bruise, cut, shock and rabies.
I heard lately that the hauteur of Melb.’s Em-
press was cultivated in the first instance at the
suggestion of her “ dear ” predecessor. That wily
diplomate confided to the young neophyte, who
sought her guidance in London, that the initial
mistake of her own colonial career had been a too
gushing demeanour, which, once inaugurated, had
to be kept up. The result, she stated, was most
annoying familiarity on the part of her subjects,
especially the dowagers who (horrid old creatures!)
actually took_ to kissing her on every possible
occasion. This explains a good deal. We under-
stand now why Somebody, though not so much as
at first, keeps her own sex at arm’s length, fight-
ing particularly shy of any stout purrer who
shows signs of wanting to clasp her in a motherly
embrace.
Ihe Princess’ Panto, bowls rather merrily along,
handicapped though it be by a partial misnomer.
The Beast, whose accents would be audible in the
midst of an earthquake, comes up to expectation,
but no one answering to the description of a
Beauty is to be seen anywhere about, and we look
back with a sigh to Vi Varley as last season’s
pretty Red Riding Hood.
The heroine of the present production is a
bridling young thing who would have been suited
well enough by the name of “Disy ” or “ Beller,”
in fact, anything at all but “ Beauty.” However,
if the pantomime star in question plumes herself
on her music-hall wriggle and a twang which puts
the “ colonial ” article to the blush, no one has
any right to dispute her possession of these
charms. Certainly she is a graceful dancer of the
saucy, piquante school.
. Beauty’s Dad, another importation, is an amus-
patriarch, as times go, but when, as the result
of a rough-and-tumble, he gives an unusually life-
like representation of certain phases of internal
upheaval, no one laughs much. Perhaps a hot
climate is not conducive to the thorough apprecia,-'
tion of that sort of joke. The flower of the*
foreigners, so far as “ Beauty and the Beast v- is
concerned, is Marie Luella, otherwise ‘-‘the ’
Prince,” though one would like the dashing
creature all the better if she hadn’t an extreme
weakness for nigger ditties. I hear Miss ,
Mane was formerly an Amurkan, which,explains,
the prevalence of banjo in her musical accompani-
ments.
Clever D’Arcy Stanfield was promptly spotted s
among the pantomimists by two- friends from!
Bananaland, who, thereupon, related that the last,
tune they had seen him was ’way up Nonh
travelling about with a piece called ‘‘Cockalorum, ” ’
or some such name. The ballet, and so on, said i
the narrators, was represented by four very tall!
stout ladies dressed as pages. The stage was.
small and low, the audience uncomfortably near.
All the bold, bad young men of the district crow-
ded into the front chairs and sat glowering and
sniggering at the dunseuses. But the fairy four
presented such a very muscular and well-trained
appearance as they defiantly returned their tor-
mentors scrutiny, that at first opportunity the
latter sauntered out, singing “ The people would
roar and roll on the floor, if they saw the old
woman in tights.” After the interval, those flash
young men were seen to have found places further
back in the building, where they sat prepared for
hasty flight if necessary.
Last summer, bright sun-bonnets were suffi-
ciently numerous to supply a dash of welcome,,
color to the sombre scenery of Victorian beaches.
This season they are epidemic. A substantial 1 /
dame, sojourning not far from Beaumaris, daily
listens to the sad sea-waves, accompanied by her
10 daughters; the whole party, from 16-stone ma
down to two-year-old Tottie, tied up in capacious
pink hoods identical in everything but size.
The Austral Salon, in the full expectancy of
female suffrage, intends indulging in political dis-
cussions. Things have not been altogether peace-
ful of late among the gushing Salonites, and the
modest members who have hitherto kept quiet
show signs of revolting against the autocracy of
one or two blatant pen-women, who have bem
running the Salon as a huge advt. for themselves
and their puny literary efforts.
It is whispered that English cricketer Phillip-
son is engaged to a Toorak heiress, the daughter
of a well-known stock salesman.
At a certain fashionable wedding, not long ago-
the rejected one went to the church, determined!
to quench her with his eagle eye. Coming down,
the aisle, she beamed on him with a gracious-
smile. He ducked, bowed, and grinned from ear
to ear. Afterwards, I am told, he was “ fit to.
bash his hat in.”

The pretty comedy of “Hans the Boat-
man ” is doing a brief season at Sydney Criterion
with rather less success than it deserves. 11,ms
with his quaint German accent, and his wildly in-
verted English, and his dog, and his songs,' and
his picturesque costume, is always welcome, and
just now, when Charles Arnold seems to be in
even better form than usual, he is more than
usually welcome. Hans and the big dog fall in a
refreshing shower on a community which is feel
mg sun-dried with much garish pantomime and.
vast, flaring melodrama. They are a curious
mixture of bright comedy and unspeakable pathos,
with enough mu«ic and dance thrown in to make
an ordinary-sized opera, and enough small children
for two or three pantomimes. Charles Arnold
may be briefly dismissed with the statement that-'
he is the only actor in Australia who could plav
Hans, and he plays that attractive person for aft
that is in him and a little more. Also, his hi.*
dog is the only dog in the-world that could piav
Hans’ dog; he is letter-perfect in his part and
performs it with much feeling. Miss Dot Frederics ■'
has improved on the character of Jeffie until I
there is little to choose between her and thaD
vanished idol, Pattie Browne, Mr. E W
Thomas, as old Yank Tbursby, is a notable sue-'
cess, and the infant, the baby coquette, and the
“kiddies” generally, are the brightest crowd of
small offspring that Sydney has seen for some
time—barring only the infant builders in RignoldY
pantomime. “Hans” is a comedy without any
trace or semblance of vulgarity, an opera with the'
high-kicking element left out, and a pantomime
with the naked element wholly omitted, and is;
hereby cordially recommended as a show that
deserves the warm support not only of every-
ordinary human man, woman, and child, but also,
of the Y.M.C.A., and the Y.W.C.A. and the
W.0.T.U., and the 1.0.G/l’., and the A N. A
and the A.S.U., and the A.L.F., and kindred in-
stitutions.


LUXURY FOR THE MASSES.
Adam Smith, the greatest economist, used to say
that one should not judge of a man’s fortune by
his expenses, but by his savings. This saying
finds its confirmation amongst all classes of the
people. However, there are luxuries which a man
does not like to deprive himself of, such as going
to the play or a concert occasionally. There are
other luxuries which grow on man, becoming habits
first and then necessities of every-day life.
Amongst these the most prevalent is smoking.
Now, if a man be wise he will take care to injure
neither his health nor his purse by indulging in it
injudiciously ; he will, therefore, look out for the
best-value smoke in the market, and by adopting
Em■■■sard's Gavour cigars he is sure to hit on the
right thing. They are mild and aromatic, and are
sold in pickets o' eight for one shilling.
A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It) has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to"all other
foods, but it had one drawback—the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection ; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone who makes
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.

EVERYBOtTLE IMPORTED INTO IN f .S.W
The greatest circus on earth— every circus
is the greatest circus on earth—was announced to
open at Redfern (Sydney), opposite the railway-
station, last Tuesday. This is Woodloch’s first
appearance in N.S. Wales, and his show—entirely
apart from the fact that it is the greatest circus
on earth —is a large and well-equipped concern.
It includes 70 horses; a gambling pony which
plays euchre and holds a remarkably good mouth-
ful of trumps; two clever troupes of acrobats,
trapezists, and contortionists ; a lady-rider who
does remarkable feats of horsewomanship and is
young and rather pleasant to look upon, besides ;
a number of trained monkeys; and many other
things. That well-known personage, Mr. Allan
Hamilton, manages the show.
** * *
“Charley’s Aunt” starts next week on a
tour of the suburbs of Sydney. On Monday the
lady from Brazil will lighten the culchawed gloom
of Manly, at the Aquarium ; and on Tuesday she
will appear at the School of Arts, North Shore, to
shed a radiance over the less patrician melancholy
of that locality. Other arrangements for the
more effectual diffusion of the gentleman in
woman’s clothes will be announced later on.
Paul Gray’s comedy company was an-
nounced, at time of writing, to open Sydney
School of Arts hall on Wednesday last with a new
comedy called “Love Without Alloy.” The
Bulletin isn’t personally acquainted with Mr.
Paul Gray, but it hereby wishes him every success
whoever he may or may not be.
** * *
The Kinetoscope is still on exhibition at
148 Pitt-street, Sydney, and sundry new pictures
have been added for the sake of variety. A
wrestling-match by the late J. K. Emmett’s big
dog is one of the present attractions. Others are
Caicedo, the wire-walker;theblacksmith’sshop with
three men hammering on the anvil; the wrestlers
two individuals who drag each other round in many
picturesque attitudes; theinfuriated cock-fight, &c.
The kinetoscope, as a means of showing all the
particulars of an assault and battery, a football
scrum, a murderous disagreement between two
dogs, a fire, or any other stirring episode, is even
more graphic than the original performance. It
has no equal as a condensed chamber of horrors,
and has innumerable points as an educational in-
fluence, a leg-show, an art exhibition, a com-
I ressed skirt-dance, a ballet, and many other
things.
** * *
Sydney Lyceum pantomime of “Cin-
derella” comes to an end at the close of next week.
At Her Majesty’s, “ The House that Jack Built ”
is still meeting with the success it deserves, and,
in view of the large business that is doing, it will
probably last another month or two, or more. G.
Rignold’s Christmas show began well, and it keeps
improving as it goes on. Bland Holt’s “ spec-
tacular marine novelty,” at the Royal, is also
prospering on a large scale, and Mr. W. E. Baker,
the manly actor with the phenomenal voice, will
continue to get up on top of the lighthouse every
night, and ring the alarm-bell over the troubled
sea as a warning to the steamer, for an undefined
period. A photo, of this talented Australian
actor —perhaps the best all-round man the country
has yet produced—appears in this issue of The
Pulletin.
** * *
The last nights of Melb. Princess’s panto,
are already announced, although the actual retire-
ment of this well-dressed affair may not happen
for another fortnight. “Beauty and the Beast ”
will doubtless philander in their platonic manner
for the Firm’s regulation period of six weeks.
Little Alma Gray, who sings of her unrequited
affection for a phlegmatic, serious-minded musi-
cian down among the orchestra, and dances with
wopderful grace, considering her inches, is an
oasis in the desert; also a baby member of the
perennial Faust Family seems to be a popular
feature of a show which, as previously remarked,
is strongest in its occasional bits of juvenile
interest.
** * *
“Aladdin,” at the Royal, continues to
pick up fresh ideas as it runs along, and the
history of last year’s success is being repeated by
Maggie Moore & Go. Widow Twankey (H. R.
Robert#) is an alert individual, with a growing
disposition to comment on passing events. A
Caledonian minion of the Chinese Emperor plays
a more useful part in the business than at first,
and several polite remarks concerning Miss
Roberts and other active supporters of the lady
superior can be accepted as read. The children’s
variety display at 8 p.m. counts for a good deal.
Likewise, the popularity of the prices, which
appeal to the tenderest part of human nature, is
thoroughly well deserved.
** * *
Elton’s broad comedy, assisted by the
joyous swettness of the young ladies who call him
“papa,” has given four weeks of life to “The
Judge,” at Melb. Bijou, but the end of that un-
real narration draws nigh. To-morrow (Friday)
Elton’s legs will make their farewell appearance,
for the present, in connection with a stage foot-
bath. They have been the main support of “ The
Judge,” those two coy limbs. When Miss Clara
Thompson draws aside the Turkish towel that
curtams them and starts drying her tears upon it,
the audience is unfeignedly happy. On Saturday
the Bijou company, slightly revised and
strengthened by the addition of John Forde and
Meta Pelham, will trot out “ The Foundling ” for
the first time in Melbourne.
** * *
Mr. and Mrs. Dacre, whose portraits are
published in this issue, will make their Australian
debut at Melbourne Bijou early next month.
Amy Roselle brings with her the reputation of
being one of the most powerful actresses in
emotional characters that the present generation
has applauded, and her husband is an equally
high-class artist. During the last six years the
Daereshave been several times Mentioned as “stars”
who would arrive shortly under engagement with
Messrs. W. &M.orß. & B. Now they are really
here, or will be by the next Orient boat, accom-
panied by some new people, and as far as any
time can be right in the present state of Aus-
tralia’s finances, this is the right time for these
whole-hearted artists to make themselves fully
appreciated. It is almost settled that the Dacres
will open in “A Wife’s Ordeal,” a play written
specially for the leading lady by G. R. Sims.

The Wild-Cat Column .
The Q.N. Bank has now only .£3,600,000 of
Government funds on hand, including £242,000 in
Treasury-notes. This is a sad state of things for the
shareholders, who not long ago had reason to hope that
the deposit would reach the round £5,000,000. Press
comment, and press comment only, has hindered Boodle-
wraith from replenishing the anxious coffers with another
thumping loan.
• * * *
The New York Life Insurance Co. has recently
had a complete audit and valuation of its securities by
the Insurance Commissioners of Massachusetts, Illinois,
and five other states of the Union. These State officials,
after a careful audit of the accounts and an examinalion
of all the various securities which go to make up the
£32,000,000 of the co.’s funds, report that there is enough
to meet all liabilities to policy-holders and other creditors,
and a magnificent surplus of £3,505,651. It would be a
good thing for Australia if the same facilities tor State
audit existed locally. A certificate of solvency from the
State auditor is worth more than dozens of certificates
from private auditors hired by the company, yet even if
an Australian society desired to submit its accounls to
public inspection in this fashion, the law provides no
method by which it can do so.
* * * *
Among the petitions presented to the Victorian
Legislative Council against the Land Tax Bill were one
from the Scottish Union and National Insurance Co., one
from the Caledonian Insurance Co., one from the North
British and Mercantile Insurance Co., all of Scotland, one
from the Norwich Union Insurance Co., one from the
London Guarantee and Accident Co., and one from the
London and Lancashire Insurance Co., these three latter
being English concerns. And the Upper House solemnly
professed to regard these six English and Scotch insurance
companies, which have hardly any property—some of
them no property at all save a small bank-balance and
some office stools—in Victoria, as being the voice of the
people, to be treated with reverence accordingly!
When the Australian nation grows a little more demo-
cratic it will shunt the foreign insurance co.’s right out of
the country on the ground that they do nothing that the
local co.’s couldn’t do quite as well, and that they send
£150,000 or £200,000 a year in profits to England for which
this suffering country gets no return whatever.
* » * *
The people of Toronto (Canada) have ascer-
tained that there are 40 foreign insurance companies
doing fire business in their city, aud that they send away
annually £140,000 in profits. To stop this exit of hard
cash the people are now thinking of establishing a muni-
cipal insurance department, and summarily ejecting the
foreign institutions. It is estimated that when the
salaries of 39 managers, and the rent of 39 offices, and
the expenses of 39 separate agency staffs are dispensed
with, the municipal institution will be able to reduce
rates one-half and still make a substantial profit.

The Trustees, Executors and Agency Co. (Mel-
bourne) pays a div. for the half-year at the rate of 7£ per
cent, per annum. The net profits for the six months
were £2265, and the amount received from rents and
interest on the invested funds of the shareholders was
12493. Therefore, the co., as in several previous half-
years, makes, to put it mildly, no profit on its legitimate
business, and its only real source of revenue is the interest
on its own invested capital and reserve fund.
• * * *
Of the N.S.W. Treasury notes, which the Dibbs
Government issued to relieve the financial crisis of ’93,
£426 worth are still afloat, despite the fact that they
have been nominally withdrawn for a year past. It will
be interesting to learn how many of these notes fail to
come back, and are never heard of again. Considering
how very few of them were issued, the limited area in
which they were circulated, and the very short time they
were in circulation, if even £25 worth ultimately drops
out of sight it will be a sufficient proof that there is a big
source of revenue in the note-issuing business.

“ Is there any reason, is there any sense of jus-
tice, is there sanity in compelling a man who lent £lO,OOO
on unproductive land to pay £4O 13s. 4d., while his neigh-
bour, who puts £lO,OOO into Government debentures, only
pays £6 13s. 4d. (C. J. Ham, estate agent, in the Vic.
Assembly.) Outside of a few reckless, fraudulent “ boom”
transactions, where is there an instance of a mortgagee
advancing £lO,OOO on unproductive land ? A bundle of
proofs of folly and roguery contains no sort of argument
against the broad land-tax principle, though it may brislle
with evidence that many Melbourne financiers should be
picking oakum instead of doing the block and hoorooing
for the Legislative Council. As well might Ham ask":
“Is there any sense of justice in taxing the income
derived from a business which only pays J per cent, on
capital, because the company which bought it was a com-
mercial swindle ?”
» * * *
Melbourne merchants who gathered in the loose
shekels of Broken Hill retailers and then quickly got out
of the risks, are now looking askance at the majority of
orders from Westralia. Stacks of dishonored Westralian
paper have been the rule in Melbourne, for months past,
but the dishonors, for obvious reasons, were kept dark.
Now that Melb. houses have got most of their money.
South Australia is welcome to the major portion of the
trade, and that province will smell Sheol if the lesson of
Broken Hill has been forgotten.
* * * *
Sydney banks, reconstructed and other, are
quietly closing numerous branches in small inland towns.
* * • •
The Yankees take ad vantage of Victoria’s pro-
tective policy. Formerly the American Tobacco Co.,
which has a modest capital of £7,000,000, exported to Vic-
toria cigarettes retailed at 6d. per packet. Now it has
bought up all the Vic. factories and started business in
Melbourne. It employs over 100 bands, produces the
same quality smokes for 3d. a packet, and sends 60 per
cent, of the profits to the land of wooden hams. Wanted,
that absentee-tax.
* * • «
A N.S.W. borough council auditor sued his em-
ployers for £3O, for services performed. A lawyer cun-
ningly raised the point that the Municipalities Act did
not order that an auditor should be paid, but only per-
mitted a local body to award any fees that it might con-
sider adequate; therefore, an auditor had no power to
recover legally any amount at which he might value his
services. A Small Debts Bench bowed to the lawyer’s
contention and gave a verdict for the borough council.
Which reminds that quite recently a municipal auditor in
N.S.W. was actually fined for “resigning” his position.
The law of municipal auditing in N.S.W. seems to be a
huge joke.

The New Zealand Fire Insurance Co. pays a
respectable div. of 12J per cent, and 2J per cent, bonus.
Its net premium income for the year was £26s,ooo—about
fnur times that of the biggest Australian fire-office—and
the profits are £45,000. The N.Z. Co. has a paid-up
capital of £200,000 and a reserve-fund of £235,000, and
can fairly claim to be the largest and most stable institu-
tion of its kind in Australasia.

♦ * * *
Thanks partly to the action of N.S.W. Govt,
in refusing to march the military on Newcastle, and so con-
vert a strike into a civil war, the coal-owners have deemed
it judicious to come to terms with their men, and the
dispute seems for the present ended.
* * * *
The Liverpool and London and Globe Co., an
English proprietary co. doing both fire and life insurance,
has started life business in N.S. Wales within the last few
days. It is a big and most reputable concern with total
funds of over £8,000,000. The management is in England,
much too far away for any Australian policy-holder to
make any serious personal investigation into its
doings; the funds are also in England ; and the
business is wholly controlled by the shareholders, who
take what share of the profits they think fit and leave the
balance to the policy-holders. Nevertheless, the L.L. and
G. pays reasonably good bonuses, and has a respectable
reputation tor liberality—and it makes one more among
the vast array of English companies which propose to ex-
ploit Australia for the benefit of the übiquitous British
shareholder. It is also worth noting that its premiums,
with a very few exceptions, are higher than those of the
A.M.P. Society, which charges the highest rates among
the big Australasian offices. Therefore, the gap between
the premiums of the Liverpool, London and Globe, and
those of such offices as the M.L. Government Department
and the Colonial Mutual, which charge bed-rock rates, is
something considerable.
• * » »
Is that abandoned shaft at Parker’s Range,
W. A., on which no work had been done for ten mouths,
identical with “the Jacoletli gold-mine” so liberally
advertised for flotation in London Daily Teleqraph of
Oct. 24? This is the shaft for the forfeiture of which J.
R. Filewood applied to the Coolgardie Warden, proving
that the labor regulations had never been complied with,
and being informed that no exemption had been granted.
Then the Warden wired to Premier John Forrest, and
received the astounding reply, “I granted exemption
personally three months ago.” And, on the strength of
this telegram, lessee Turnbull was maintained in posses-
sion by the Warden. The question was simply—Were the
goldfields regulations complied with ? Admittedly they
were not. Then why was the forfeiture not granted?
The Bulletin doesn’t know ; but here are the names of
the “ advisory committee in Australia ” of “ the Jacoletti
gold-mine J. Matthew Turnbull, Alexander Forrest
(Premier Forrest’s “little brother”), John Turnbull.”
Poor Filewood!

• • • •
Londonderry shares, writes The Bulletin’s
London correspondent by last mail, do not go over well in
the market, and at, present are at a sixteenth dis. They
are now and then “ faked up” to par, but as soon as any
of the underwriting crowd try to slip a few shares down
they slump. This will make the Wealth of Nations deal
difficult to engineer, and North and Co. will have
to be contented with a much smaller grab than they
had out of the Londonderry. A friend of mine sub-
scribed—against my strong advice—for 250 shares, as he
was told by some interested person that he would only
have a few allotted to him, but to his disgust he was
landed with the lot. So much for letters of regret. I
don’t believe that the public took £25,000 worth of shares.

lb is rather strange that the most wholesome

An American mining journal describes North’s
Londonderry deal as the most impudent ever attempted,
and as one which could not be carried through by any
other man in London. Cost, £lBO,OOO and 116,000 shares
for the owners of this small-area mine ; £50,000 working
capital; £354,000 in cash and shares, profits from the re-
sale of the property ; total, £700,000. These figures
must have taken the public’s breath away, for it is under-
stood that not £lOO,OOO was subscribed. The under-
writers were left with stacks of shares, and with so many
cheap scrip in the market they were soon offered at a
discount. The thing was badly manoeuvred. Taking one
consideration with another the original owners’ lot
£lBO,OOO cash and 116,000 shares, is a happy one. Their
executive partner ia/a smart financier, and has handled
his mine excellently. The Londonderry is a flittering
show, but it is circumscribed and undeveloped, and may
be worth a million or not a tenth of it.

The Llanberis Co., East Ballarat, has for years
paid enormous divs.—all obtained from the royalty
demanded of tributors-the co. itself doing little more
than hoisting the flag for the monthly “ divide.” Another
local claim pays heavy royalty (£30,000 to date) to the
lease-holder, who never put apick into the ground. There
are several others in the same position. It the gold in a
lease belongs to the Crown, why should a successful
shepherd get it?

Steele’s Creek, 40 miles from Melbourne, near
Yarra Glen, has proved a good poor man’s diggings so far.
The sinking is 20 to 40 feet, and the-wash about 2 feet. It
was kept quiet for a while, but now the thing is out and a
rush will probahly make it a very poor man’s diggings.

A Bulletin correspondent: Garangula, N.S.W.,
plods along steadily—finds work and a living for upwards
of 400 persons. It is believed to have yielded about
2000 oz. gold since last June, and the same claims are still
working. The dirt is carted to the washing-machine
and put through by the miners, at a total cost of
Is. 6d. per load. Mr. Macansh, the freeholder, has
never enforced 1 rent, although legally entitled to do
so. Since Xmas holidays, 147 loads have been washed
up by seven parties for an average of about 9dwts.
A battery has been erected at Garangula
and about a dozen claims have stone at grass.
The only quartz worked in the vicinity until now was the
old Marshal M'Mahon reef, which had costly machinery
and was abandoned because the rich shoot pinched out.
The shows now working are similar and exhibit gold in
shoots and veins from 6in. to 2ft. with good walls" goino-
down straight and running N.E. and S.W. The stone
shows gold to the naked eye, and is thick with mineral.
The miners are hoping for a visit from the Mines Depart-
ment expert.
* • * *
The Shoalhaven Consolidated Sluicing Co. has
passed out with a liquidation div. of 2d. 19-90ths. Why,
will always be a bit of a mystery to the paid-up Sydney
shareholders. There were no debts, and no liabilities, and
the result of the last washing showed a profit. It is
registered and managed in Adelaide, where the property
is still owned.
* * » *
Sunlight, Hillgrove, making extensive prepara-
tions for better working of the mine and more effective
battery-work concentration. Above all, the lode is carry-
ing better gold. It is time the shareholders had some
return for the expenditure of a heap of money.

A returned Westralian gold-seeker avers that
the food served to the saloon passengers in his boat on
the eastward trip was badly cooked and almost unfit for
consumption, and even when Adelaide was reached, no
fresh supplies were taken in. The only two kinds of meat
on board appeared to be “fresh” lamb and corned beef.
The beef could he smelt far to windward, and the lamb
was generally served half raw and not sufficiently “done ”
to destroy the maggots.
(“ Wild Gat ” continued on page 23).

A correspondent:—“To Cran, of Mil-
laquin (Q.), who died some short time
ago, must be awarded the palm of honor.
Although a planter and a mill-owner, he never
victimised an employe for his political opinions.
His hands were Labor men almost to a man, and
not one of them was ever made to suffer for his
convictions.”
At the Xmas breaking-up of the New
England Grammar School, Armidale, Bishop
Green, who presided, said that during his travels
through the diocese he came across a well-educated
man, who informed him that the only literature
he (the well-educated man) read was The Bulletin
and Dr. Talmage’s sermons. “Which was the
antidote and which the poison I really cannot
say,” remarked his reverence.

The controversy as to who was the first
Mayor of Sydney is all the more unintelligible
seeing that there are marble slabs on one of the
main staircases in the City Hall, whereon are in-
scribed in gilt letters the names of all the aider-
men who have held the office of Mayor since John
Ilosking, in 1812. Thirty eight alderman have at
various times occupied the chair during the city s
52 years of incorporation. John Harris was
Mayor five times, Manning, Merriman and Moure
three times each, Thurlow, Chapman, Palmer,
Sydney Burdekin and Riley twice each
Brisbane has Brunton Stephens, Essex
Evans, Arthur Bayldon, Francis Kenna, “ Halek”
Nicholson, Meston, and many other really good
writers, whose combined earnings from the
Queensland press would not reach .£5O a year.
Yet, when a young poet flaps his wings, the lead-
ing papers criticise him, and deplore the lack of
literary development in Queensland and the bad
times which are ruining the owners of the dailies.
Reporters at from 15s. to 255. a week are calculated
to starve-out journalists and send writers to other
markets.

The just-demised Judge 0. B. Skinner
(Melb.) was an out-of-the-way man. He was
brought up as heir to one of the largest English
fortunes, but his father died of a broken heart,
after dropping his all in Venezuela silver mines,
so young Skinner had to go to work. He made a
name in India as a barrister, one of the first to
plead in a native language. Another celebrated
Skinner in India at the time, no relative, was Co).
Skinner, who had 12 native wives. C. 8., coming
to Melbourne with cash, became a useful land-
jobber, and laid out and named the suburb of
Malvern. A boom-burst drove him to accept a
county-court judgeship.
J. H. Dalziell, M.P., writes to Hogan, M.P.,
who leaves Sydney for England this week, saying
that he intends to tour Australia when the British
Parliament rises. Dalziell is one of the few men
who have risen to eminence in politics in England
straight from the position of a newspaper reporter.
He was Parliamentary representative of the
Scottish Leader, and the editor of that paper
thought he would get in front of his rival, the
Scotsman, if he had a correspondent in the
House. Accordingly the whips were asked to find
a seat for Dalziell. They found him one in Scot-
land, and he has made his mark since he has been
in the Commons. Gladstone has paid warm com-
pliments to the strenuous young Radical.
A very few months of wrestling with Vic-
toria’s finances have sufficed to shatter the nerves
and sour the temper of Premier Turner. He is
one more argument for the theory that a man
must become inured to political life and
“party” struggles in his young days if he is to
carry the responsibility of leadership without
breaking down. The “hard, common-sense”
persons who enter Parliament in their middle age,
and try to be fairly honest when they get there,
almost invariably come to physical grief if they
force themselves, or allow themselves to be
dragged into leading positions. They may sit in
the odour of politics without danger to their
health, but when they get right into the stink of
Ministerialism they soon collapse.
The late N.S. W. ex-Chief Justice Stephen,
writes an Anglo - Australian from London to
The Bulletin, used, in all cases where he thought
that he might have taken too severe a view of a
prisoner’s case, to postpone sentence until he had
consulted his wife, to whom he read the whole of
his notes of evidence. “He told this to me,” con-
tinues the correspondent, “and added: ‘ I have
many times escaped making a mistake by that
means, and I never know her to be wrong in her
judgment, which I frequently tested by enquiries
made privately. Nor did she always err on the
side of mercy, but took a just and impartial view
of every case on its merits.’ The Judge,” adds
the writer, “was occasionally severe, but he was
always conscientious. He had a theory that a
man who had committed a great offence never
reformed, and that the Discharged Prisoners’
Societies did a great deal of harm. He carefully
scrutinised the demeanour of witnesses, and
looked upon a defence supported by doubtful
evidence as a strong presumptive proof of the
guilt of the person in whose behalf it was given.
With all his faults and peculiarities, which occa-
sionally made him appear petty, he was a great
and a just Judge, and it will be long ere you look
upon his like again.”

The opponents of the lately-passed S.A.
progressive land and absentee taxes tried, by
petition, to persuade Gov. Kintore that the Act

The Vic. Govt., being unable to make
terms with the P. & O. and Orient Co.’s re
freights, will shortly call for tenders for the
carriage of the province’s products to British and
other markets. This will burst up the shipping
combine, which, in grasping at too much, will
lose all.

The Australian Tory catechism. “ What
is the M.P. who votes for Payment of Members?
A Sturdy Beggar.” “What is the M.L.O. who
votes against his own land being taxed ? A Pure
and Holy Patriot, a Champion of the People’s
Liberties, and a police patrol for the Sacred Rights
of Property.”
The explanation of the fact that land-
taxation is opposed chiefly by—in Victoria, Free-
traders, and in N.S.W., Protectionists —lies in the
fact that party politics have made Legislative
Councillors the temporary political abettors of—•
in Victoria, the Freetraders, and in N.S. Wales,
the Protectionists.
A Jap boom is now the exploiting feat
which the British pawnbroker intends to try on
Australia. London Times puts the scheme
bluntly ; “If Japan Can offer both labor and
markets to the (Australian) sugar industry, there
is no reason why it should not do the same for
other forms of agricultural production,” But
why only agricultural production?
The Australian Star defends the Vic.
Council’s rejection of the land and income-tax
with the plea that that eloquent fossil “represents
four-fifths of the community in simple numbers
and 99 per cent, probably of its educated intel-
ligence.” The Council, as it happens, represents
just 162,000 adult males out of about 350,000. If
the Star didn’t know a little tiling like that it is
a foolish and illiterate rag winch isn’t fit to
instruct the public. And if it did know it, then
it is a good and truthful paper which is sure to go
to heaven when it dies.

members fighting the measure to reduce their pay

The Municipal Council of Aberdeen (Scot-
land) proposes that the drink-traffic should be
managed by the municipality, and will probably
apply for Parliamentary authority to become the
sole grog-seller in the city. The Public Drink
Department keeps coming.
“A coalition in the sense of a patriotic
combination of all parties to pull the country
through its troubles” is mooted by the Age, and
pronounced blessed, as “a real advance upon
party-government.” Now, a coalition, as we
know it, is party-government in its grossest and
most corrupt form. And daily-paper patriots are
generally politicians who confound amor patriot
with amourpropre,
A week or two ago The Bulletin men-
tioned that Premier Turner was tired of his post.
He has not the soul to ruin a fine private business
for the sake of politics, and perceives that the
game is for the adventurers only. In short, he
would be glad of a decent pretence to retire, and
get back to his sarcastic legal partner, Sam
Lyons, who wants to lay to his book. His vigorous
health is in danger of being shattered. He is a

Schey, M.P. (N.S.W.), on being twitted
with his apparent inconsistency in tirading
against the issuing of free passes for the railways,
and yet applying for one for his wife, candidly
admitted the principle to be wrong, but added
that, so long as it was tolerated, he “ did not see
why he should not take the benefit of it along
with others.” It was ever thus. Those who are
most blatant in their cries for reform generally
take full advantage of existing abuses.
A possibility of £ £ Smith getting back
into the Assembly hangs over Victoria like a—
suppose we say, a rainbow in the sky. The
Mornington election—which £ £ lost by a nose, a
neck between second and third—has been declared
void, and the mercurial doctor may perhaps
regain his Paradise after all. But Bourke, who
polled only 22 votes behind Smith last time, ought
to do the trick if he tries again. Downward, the
disqualified member, has proved himself a hard-
shell Tory, whilst Bourke is the only land-taxer of
the trio.
Australian Governors are expected to keep
the Home Office apprised of all important changes
in the body politic in their charge. They conse-
quently find it necessary to occasionally state that
So-and-So’s letter to the Times merely echoes an
individual opinion, and an individual of small
consequence, to boot. Some great little men
should bear this in mind. When it came to
Parkes’ knowledge on one occasion that a N.S.W.
Governor had so communicated concerning certain
opinions of his he became unreasonably excited ;
but while Governors are imported such things
must happen.
Bishop Thornton, of Ballarat, addressing
Vic. National Scripture Education League :
The Church of Rome was doing what the other Christian
churches neglected to do—grasping hold of their children.
Why were conversions so rare amongst Roman Catholics?
Simply because their religious education was looked after
in their youth, and thus a sort of artificial conscience
was created which made them refuse to even listen to
attempts at proselytism.
It is good to know exactly what the pietists want
to bestow upon the children. Even Samuel Bal-
larat will be content when every youngster is
fitted out with an “ artificial conscience”—which,
by the way, would do credit to Voltaire as a
definition of religion.

Barrister Higgins, M.P. (V.), will be

Ministry down South. He votes consistently
with the party, and is a genuine and most highly-
cultnred Democrat. He is the only lawyer out
of 13 who voted against the second reading of
Turner’s Reduced Payment of Members Bill.
Also, he is the Radical and reformer of the
University Council.

The Bishop of Salis-
bury (Eng.), a peripatetic
prelate, preaching in Mel-
bourne recently, said that
Victoria, in persisting in
her secular system of edu-
cation, “is creating an
artificial and hollow peace,
which may some day be
shivered to a thousand
fragments by an explosion
of selfish passion.” This
is the average parson’s
apology for his miserable
existence. When he dis-
covers a country that does
not regard him as a press-
ing necessity, he either
insists that that country
is a sink of iniquity, or
prophesies that it is about
to develope all the horrors
of a combined Sodom and
Gomorrah, with up-to-date
improvements. Australians
have no faith in reverend
prophets, and few of these
latter have faith in them-
selves. Those who have, in-
variably leave the pulpit
for the stock-exchange.

FLETCHER'S PILLS are a quick, sure and

TO THE HON. LEGISLATIVE COUNCIL, &C.
We beg to state, with deep respect,
That broken be our backs will
Unless you solemnly reject
The Land and Income Tax Bill.
Pa told us what we ought to say—
His grammar is hewild’ring ;
So “chuck ” that Bill, we humbly pray.
Signed, Three of Fat Man’s Childring.
I write from Muggerwugger shire,
And as a favor ask it—
Please put that bill upon the fire,
Or drop it in your basket.
It isn’t that I don’t agree
With Turner’s propositions;
But, here’s the point : my mortgagee
Is working up petitions.
Poor Farmer.
A busted bank-manager I,
A friend of all frauds and abuses ;
I therefore take part in the cry
Which proper taxation produces,
And state, at your urgent request,
That the sight of a land-taxing measure
Gives Capital pains on the chest,
And moves me to instant displeasure.

A delicate little misunderstanding arose
concerning the public demonstration in Adelaide
t<> bid farewell to Gov. Kintore. The Mayor con-
vened a meeting of citizens to consider the matter,
and the usual ousequious group of rich Conserva-
tives attended. They carried a resolution that
the valedictory gathering should be in the Town-
ball, and that Lieut.-Gov. Way should preside.
The Mayor, however, firmly declined to grant the
Town-hall for a public meeting of citizens unless
he was to take the chair, and the Municipal
Council loyally upheld him in his very proper
determination.

An illustration of the relative importance
of things in Australia, just now. Premier King-
ston, who is an ambitious statesman and an
enthusiastic cricketer, had the conference of
Premiers, at Hobart, postponed to enable him to
see the third test cricket-match in Adelaide !

If members of Parliament may deadhead
their wives over the railway lines of Australia,
may women, when they become members, similarly
deadhead their husbands? It is to be hoped that
the first woman elected in South Australia will be
married, and will put the “ principle ’’ in practice,
so that the whole continent may obtain an oppor-
tunity of jumping to its feet and stamping the
system out o existence.

The publication of that list of free-passes,
issued to the “wives” of N.S.W. M.’sP., has
ciusedmuch gnashing of teeth. One law-maker
is credited with obtaining a pass for his “bitter-
half,” though the latter has very material evidence
to prove that on the date mentioned it was a
physical impossibility for her to leave home.
Another legislator, who was generally supposed
to be a bachelor, is now being shunned by the
hopeful girls of his acquaintance.
# * *
Delicious rigmarole from “Counterblast
to Spookland.” Dr. M‘Carthy is trying to explain
why his so-called spirit-photographs are some-
thing quite different :
“ But how do you explain it, doctor?”
“Very easily ; I had been some days previously think-
ing a good deal about my dead friend and had actually
been trying some sketches of his face which was, so to
speak, photographed on the tablets of my memory. I
had, lo he sure, forgotten all about the face during the
seance, hut the idea was taken from subliminal conscious-
ness telophatically and spontaneously by the sensitive,
when I was in the best condition for such an occurrence,
that is to say non-expectant. It was then proj cted and
objectified (materialised) under subtle sympathetic in-
fluence by means of the psychoplasm proceeding from the
body of the sensitive, but the fiaure was a mere automaton,
hearing temporary life manifestations from the medium,
and was in no way connected with the spirit of my
departed friend. It was simply an evolved thought-form,
taking on certain characteristics in facial expression, &c.,
through sympathetic force, and materialised through the
medium’s special power.”
Hjw charmingly explicit!
# • •
“A Counterblast fco Spookland ” will not
be very pleasant reading for Mr. T. S. Henry, the
head and front of the Mellon spook-exposure,
whose “treachery to the cause” which be formerly
loved is made the subject of much unkind ridicule.
There are also pictures of an individual, presum-
ably Mr. Henry, gliding like a snake in the grass
into the holy affections of “Josephine,” together
with a poem to the same lady, written by Mr.
Henry in his raw and credulous days ; and another
picture showing a wild man, also presumably Mr.
Henry, digging in Dr. M'Carthy’s back-yard for
the imaginary body of a foully-murthered citizen.
Altogether, the spooks score heavily.

This is the season at which the mailbags of
each Australian Ministry are stuffed with letters
recommending the appointment of Prominent
Citizen, Esquire, of Everywhere, as J.P. Con-
sidering the present brand of that gentleman, how
ho sprouts, and that it would he scarcely possible
to find a worse job-lot of him, and considering
also the present hard-up-ness of all the Australian
Treasuries, would it be a good or a bad idea to
license Jaypees? Let every man who has an
ambition to retail so-called Justice send in his
application, accompanied by a specified fee, and
take his chance. If even the small degree of care
were taken with respect to such applicants that is
taken with regard to applicants for liquor-victual-
ing, hawkers’, tobacco, milk, or other licenses, the
odds would be in favor of a better average Jaypee
being struck than by the present method of
nomination as a reward for political (that is,
party) services rendered. -And if penalties were
provided for the commission of offences which
might be comprehensively scheduled under some
such generic term as “ unjaypee-like conduct,”
petty “Justice” as she is now scandalised might
be improved upon. “The Great Unpaid ” would
at least give place to the Great Paying-for-the-
honor.
• « «
A story of life and death. In Victorian
public life there was a certain undertaker. One
day, while he stood at a festive board surrounded
by notables, their champagne-glasses raised aloft,
a telegram was slipped into his bands. It was
from his wife, who, in his absence, attended to
the details of the business, and ran thus : “ Can’t
get Smith into coffin, his knees are stiff —what
shall we do ?” The undertaker set down his glass,
turned up the corner of the telegram, and wrote
with a stumpy pencil, “ Cut his sinews.” It
was done. Such is life. You can’t always tell
what a man is thinking of when he flourishes his
glass and drinks your very good health.
A woman’s great mission in life is to make
her dress hang straight, and to keep her pocket
from bulging and spoiling the look of things; con-
sequently when she has any luggage to carry she
has to stow it about her person in all sorts of un-
expected places where it won’t stick out. On a
recent Saturday night a Sydney lady, with a
bottle of medicine in her stocking, fell while run-
ning after a tram, and drove the broken bottle so
effectually into her system that she had to be
taken to the hospital to have it extracted. Also,
the medicine got into her wounds and contusions,
and aggravated things considerably. A month
earlier another lady who had bestowed a bottle of
scent in the bosom of her dress, fell out of an
omnibus, and eight pieces of scent-bottle had to
be picked out of her snowy bust with tweezers.
Woman will never be the equal of man until she
has ten pockets in different parts of her clothes
and can find them without walking round herself
six times and then retiring to her bedroom and
undressing herself to look for them.
* * *
The bankruptcy of “So-and-so, gentle-
man,” is wired from Brisbane. “Gentleman!”
old Judge Noel, of Melb. Bankruptcy Court, used
to exclaim when his eye spotted this designation
on a schedule. “Gentleman! gentleman!” And
glaring around the solicitors’ table in search of the
assignee he would say, “ Call him a man !” Poor
old Moel didn’t believe a man without money could
be a gentleman, and as far as in him lay tried to
set the world right on the subject.
* * *
Coroner Youl, of Melbourne, who is grow-
ing old, and isn’t able to go and “view the body”
with the agility of former years, wishes to have
this ancient formula dispensed with altogether.
Considering, however, that the jury goes and
view's the body with the very reasonable object of
making quite sure there is a body, the force of
logic goes w ith the old system, and the probabili-
ties are that the jury will go and see the remains
as of old. Coroner Ghoul, as ah'eady mentioned,
is getting old, and his work grows no lighter.
Last year’s Melb. morgue statistics included 38
suicides, 125 accidental deaths, 8 wilful murders,
25 infanticides, 26 found-deads, 4 manslaughters,
4 executions, 1 justifiable homicide, and 74 myste-
rious deaths among infants. A man with such a
year’s work as that might well grow tired of view-
ing the body.
* * *
The long-windedness of legal processes is
exemplified in the investigation into the Awhina-
Princess collision in Poit Jackson. The parties
to this collision will, before all is over, have to
appear before a coroner’s court, a police court, a
Marine Board and a Supreme Court, the whole or
almost the whole of the evidence being gone over
anew before each Bench. Afterwards there may
be civil actions, which will also call for the bulk
of the same witnesses with the same statements.
* * #
Applicants for a N.S.W. country editor-
ship, lately vacant, were invited to “ state reli-
gious beliefs.” One out-at-elbows pressman
replied: “My journal is my religion—while the
ghost walks.” The journal in question isn’t his
religion, so far.
• * #
Sydney D.T., of Doc. 25, prints an absurdly
tall account of the scholastic exploits of a certain
lady who lately returned to Sydney with British
medical qualifications. Whatever the lady’s
British diplomas may be (they are given as
Licentiate of the Society of Apothecaries, London,
and L.R.C.P. and S., Glasgow) it would appear
that she did not also graduate at Sydney Uni-
versity, though from the Telegraph’s account the
contrary might easily be inferred. In printing its
story about the doctress in question being the
“ first N.S.W. lady to graduate with high honors
in England ” the Telegraph pays a very poor
compliment to the girls who stayed in their own
country and successfully faced, not a mere three
years’ curriculum, but one of the most arduous
and comprehensive five-years’ courses of study in
the world.
# • •
The flock of a parson, who has of late been
distinguishing himself, have long since ceased to
ask each other, “Are you going to church?”
Instead, they simply inquire, “ Are you going to
hear about Adam and Eve?” Try how lie will,
that poor parson can’t keep the fable of our spare-
clad ancestors out of his sermons. Seized
with the beauty of the story’s simplicity, especi-
ally in the matter of costumes, he (so goes
the story) a short time back attired himself in
nothing in particular, and started a Garden of
Eden in his own backyard, himself in the rdle of
Adam. He kept hard at it for some days, when he
began to feel lonely, so he tried to induce his wife,
whom he called Eve, to come and keep him com-
pany in his Eden, She refused and threatened
to call the police ; but even the wild incongruity
of a policeman in the Garden failed to startle the
unhappy parson back to his senses.
* # #
That barbarous institution, a “surprise-
party,” was lately tendered to a Sydney pawn-
broker, who, ere allowing the guests to take
possession, exacted from them 10s. cash for the
use of the room. It takes a Melchisedec or a
Cohen to rise to such occasions.

A Melb. Presbyterian clergyman of un-
doubted piety (and venom), advocates bicycling
for girls, because bicycling has made the ’Murkan
girl “stronger in body, clearer in mind, and purer
inspirit.” The “ meenister ” has in preparation
a sermon called “To Heaven on a Bicycle.” The
rev. gentleman doesn’t say wh her it is possible
for a girl to go to glory in knickerbockers.
« * *
N.S.W. educational system provides for
Godliness but not for cleanliness; it allows the
parson to upset the arrangement of the classes by
giving, once a week, instruction in his particular
brand of religion to a mere handful out of each
class in the school, but it leaves such a small
matter as the pupils’ cleanliness to the care of the
teachers, who frequently need to supply soap and
towels out of their own pockets. By the way,
teachers complain that the visiting parson’s hope-
less failure to turn tip at the appointed time
greatly interferes with the carrying out of the
school’s regulation programme.
* * *
The surest test of a girl’s character is a
trip ’Ome per average passenger steamer, especially
if she be confided to the care of the doctor and
first officer. They’ll see that the damsel is pro-
perly tested.
« » •
The “unemployed” at Shea’s Creek
(Sydney) Govt, relief works are only, in future,
to get one week’s work in every five in lieu of
one week in four, as hitherto. By the way, a
“leading” Sydney dady lately printed an article
concerning the works, from a special reporter,
setting forth the artifices used by the unemployed
in their eagerness to get more than the regulation
share of work. One poor wretch who had read in
the papers that a man employed at the works had
been injured and had gone to the hospital, promptly
put in an appearance and, impersonating the
disabled man, took his place in the gang and did
his work. But he was found out and got no pay.
Shaving and assuming another name; begging the
work-tickets of their mates who were ill or had
had the luck to get other and more remunerative
employment—these tricks, and many others, were
recounted in the special article of a newspaper
which daily says, or insinuates, that “the un-
employed don’t want work !”

Vic. detective Lomax, who died suddenly
last week, figured in the Vic. Missing Mace en-
quiry as the officer who told a Melb. editor that
The Bulletin had announced the bauble’s where-
abouts. The Mace Board was very severe on
Lomax for confirming “a libellous rumour in a
scandalous foreign newspaper,” but, somehow, the
worthy man didn’t get called upon for an apology.
Bluff fxad to be exercised with discretion in the
maoe matter.

Dear Bulletin, —As an etymologian,
“Worry M.” has a tendency towards the far-
fetched. For instance—“ Gone bung.” “Wurry’s”
billabong idea won’t do. “ Gone bung ” means
“burst up.” When a Chinese cracker bursts it
“ goes bung ! ! !” “ Push ” is derived by inference
from “crowd,” in connection with which “ push-
ing ” is inseparable. A gang of larrikins “pushes”
its way along a path, jostling pedestrians as it
goes. “Bart,” from “barter,” being applied to
“one who barters ” her charms. It is not larri-
kinese, but English “flash.” Here are a few
additional derivations of interest; —“Mnlty,”
from “ moulty,” as applied to birds ; “ tart,” from
“sweetheart,” the terminal t of “sweet” being
contracted with “heart,” illustrating a tendency
well-known to philologists, as in English “a nag,”
from Danish “an og,” a horse. Finally, here are
four for solution :—“Kybosh,” “nark,” “grig,”
and “quid” (a pound). —P.M.

Dear Bulletin, The Japs on the
Northern plantations are treated much better
than white folks. For instance, on A plan-
tation the men all get black sugar—sometimes so
bad that you can see the molasses running out of
the bag. The Japs stood this for a week or two;
then, seeing that the co. was making white sxxgar
in the mill, they struck work, and did not resume
until they got best white sugar. The whites are
still using the molasses. The Japs are not to be
sneezed at as far as holding out for their rights is
concerned. - S UGA r Miner. Mackay, Q,, Dec. fi.
# # ♦
Attempts are being made to explain the
numerous Mackay (Q.) murders on the vendetta
principle. In the New Hebrides a tribesman’s
death demands a tribesman’s death. All white
men (to the Kanaka understanding) are of one
tribe. It is possible, then, that the Mackay
outrages are simply acts of vengeance for the
many hitherto unavenged murders by blackbirders
visiting the islands.

Mr. W. E. BAKER, the talented Australian
Actor .
Chemists and grocers up North are at
loggerheads. The chemists are retailing tea (on
which they make a handsome profit) by way of
retaliation on the grocers, who have been vending
patent medicines at “ ridiculously cheap prices.'’
“Ridiculously cheap,” in the druggists’ lexicon,
probably means anything less than 200 per cent,
profit.
# # •
The wicked action of N.S. W. Government
in permitting unwholesome liquor to go out of
bond gets forcible comment from the Licensing
Guardian. Next day, perhaps, an ignorant publi-
can is fined for selling the same stuff, and is ruined
commercially. Yet the State, charging a heavy
duty of 14s. per proof gallon, is the real gainer
through the scandalous transaction.

Russian subjects of the Czar in Melbourne
swore allegiance last week. The oath consists
mainly of one big sentence of 300 words, which
the Herald thinks remarkable. But we can’t all
condense our oaths like managing-editor S. V.
Winter.

“ Australian natives are the soberest in the
world,” said a Sydney police-inspector last week.
“ The larrikin and his donah almost always go for
fruit essences, ginger ales and ice-creams. It is
the Irish and Scotch strains that account for the
heavy consumption of spirits.”

A couple of Melbourne spielers, who lately
went to exploit Westralia, had a dispute with
some Ninety-Mile miners, who referred the
matter to Judge Lynch. His Honor clipped the
gentlemen’s ears, and they are back in Melbourne
again.

The S.M. Herald is making the dangerous
experiment of saying what it really thinks about a
large section of its most solemn readers. “ Many,”
it writes, “who shout most loudly to shut tip
public-houses on Sunday are the first to find their
way into the side-doors of those places on Sunday
evenings.”

connection with a certain public department,
resulting in the dismissal, so far, of two highly-
paid officials. One of the consequences of the
exposui’e is that a firm of contractors has been
ordered to take up and relay the whole of £20,000
worth of work.

THE IDOL OF THE HOUR .
The Australasian gives a series of illus-
trations of a rational-dress picnic that went from
Mel I lourne into the leafy wilderness recently. A
casual survey of the ladies’ “ knickers ” shows that
they must be bad things to sit on a bull-ant in.
# * *
One of the saved saloon passengers of the
Wairarapa gave a little thanksgiving picnic to a
few Sydney friends the other Sunday. The party
proceeded in a steam-launch from Circular Quay
to a quiet spot in Middle Harbor, where pulling-
boats awaited them. Everything passed off well
till they were returning, when it was found that
one of the boats leaked so badly that it had to be
abandoned. As the sculls had mysteriously dis-
appeared from another, the remaining boat had
to tow the whole party back to the steamer, which
the picnickers boarded after nothing worse than
the incident of a lady falling into the water and
being rescued by two gentlemen. But their
troubles were not quite over. As the tender
rounded Middle Head a stiff “southerly ” sprang
up, and when they stepped ashore at the Quay,
almost wet through, they fervently vowed they
would never again accept the hospitality of a
saved shipvvreckite.
# * «
Sydney unemployed were refused the
Town Hall to meet in, but, to justify the Mayor,
the daily papers had to publish the resolutions at
length and anathematise them. So the agitators
gained fuller publicity than their meeting would
have received, and they ran no risk of being
jugged as anarchists!

“ No, I regret that I am unable to increase
your salary,” said a Melb. business man to bis
clerk when importuned for a rise; “bub I’ll tell
you what I will do—l’ll let you have a partner-
ship.”
# • #
A Melbourne church that originally ob-
tained a State-grant of city land for nothing, now
lets, at a big rental, the buildings on the site,
occupied respectively by a brewery, a distillery, a
tobacco-factory, and a money-lender. The ready
excuse is :“ We can’t help it. We sinned ignor-
antly, and can’t undo it. It’s let on a building
lease.”

Editor Bulletin. —Sydney Daily Tele-
graph used up three columns of print to awaken
the N.S.W. public to the fact that the female
relations of Ms.P. and others were deadheads on
the State railways. The same issue (as every
other issue) of the Daily Telegraph was carried
hundreds of miles up-country by a morning train
specially laid on to accommodate the morning
daily papers. How much special subsidy per
annum does the D.T. receive from the State rail-
ways through this one daily favor alone? —0. B.
Harland.

* * *
Garden Island, in Sydney harbor, the fruit-
ful source of bickering between naval authorities
and aesthetic citizens, never really belonged to the
people. A Government order, issued in 1841,
directed that the island was to be considered a
part of Government House domain, and that all
the growth and produce Of the island, whether
timber or grass, was to be appropriated to the
exclusive use of the Governor’s establishment.
Fines and other punishments were inflicted for
wanton trespass.

At Westport (M.L.) the New Year was
ushered in by firing an old, rusted four-pounder,
which was saved from a wreck many years ago.
Result : One burst gun, one corpse, one old man
without an arm, one captain with his ribs stove
in, three shops and two houses badly damaged,
one sehoolhouse perforated, one chimney knocked
down, one badly-scared gunner, one probable
orphan, one Happy New Year.
# • #
Melbourne suburbs are rapidly becoming
electro-lighted, with the effect of utterly defeating
the purposes for which the parks and gardens
were planted. Dozens of tired couples wander
round of an evening trying to find a seat or corner
out of the glare.

Cruickshank, M.P. (N.S.W.), in March next.

A Melb. clergyman’s sad reflections :
“It is a fact that our young men do not run after the
modest, simple, stay-at-home maiden. The smart,
‘stylish’ girl, who disregards domestic affairs, and seeks
pleasure here and there and everywhere, is the girl who
marries first.”
Sea bathing is the best recipe for keeping
the hair on, and sailors are seldom bald because
they have few opportunities to wear their hats in-
doors.
