Ab Deeringullen, near Goulburn (N.S.W.),
a dog dropped upon the canvas of a buckeye
reaper-and-binder that was being worked in a
harvest field. Before anybody could interfere,
the dog was carried by the elevators to the binding-
table, tied up in a sheaf of wheat, bound and
tossed out ! When the sheaf was unfastened, the
dog walked stupidly out, quite unhurt.
Father Curran, the geologist, was lately
nominated for a fellowship at St. John’s R.O.
College, Sydney. A certain Dean was marched
out to oppose him, but finding that the geologist
was bound to go in with a big majority the Dean
retired. However, the church has a say in the
matter, and it appears to have been arranged that
the Dean is to “get there just the same,” whether
the graduates like it or not.
Samuel Deering, late assistant - Agent-
General for S. A., got his C.M.G. on New Year’s
Day by “working the Colonial Office.” The
Kingston Govt, feel very wroth with him because
he threw up his position and claimed compensa-
tion just when the late John Bray’s illness was
most acute, and when the services of Deering
were most needed in London. Some criticisms
by the S. A. Assembly as to the cost of the Agent-
General’s office, and a characteristically brutal
threat by then Treasurer Play ford to retrench
him, are supposed to have piqued Deering, who
on retirement was entitled to eight months’ leave
on full pay and a solatium of .£2400. So it came
about that for runny weeks the S.A. Agent-
General’s office was left to the mercies of a clerk.
Dear Bulletin,— -Your paragraph re George
Grey and the fussy bush storekeeper seems to have
been “made to order,” as Grey, so far as I am
aware, never attempted to address the Maoris in
their own language. So far from being the best
speaker among the M.L. white population, he had
but a very imperfect acquaintance with the Maori
language—a knowledge acquired mainly through
intercourse with half-castes of the native race. I
remember hearing Grey about 14 years ago ad-
dressing a Maori meeting at the Karainu, a settle-
ment in Hawkes Bay, and he spoke in English,
John White having to act as interpreter for him.
This White was one of our best Maori scholars,
and ab that time was editor of a Maori news-
paper (the WhakaWhaka), published in Hawkes
Bay. Even Donald M’Lean, who was the most
prominent Native Minister, and best Maori
speaker in any Government, did not venture to
address in the Maori language the large and im-
portant gathering of natives held at Ngarua-
whaia, in the Waikato, just previously to Governor
Bowen’s departure. He spoke in English.—Yours,
&c., Huia.
[Grey, in the preface to his book, “Polynesian Mytho-
logy, ’’ states that he commenced the study of the Maori
language prior to 1850, so that he might Psten to the
petitions of the natives in their own tongue. W. H.
Taipara, the great chief of the Thames natives, once told
the writer that Grey, Dr. Shortland, and James Mackay,
junior, were the only Europeans who could speak fluently
the old classic language, as it was spoken before its
corruption by the introduction of English words, and
Wetere te Rerenga paid him a similar compliment,
although the Ngatimaniapoto chief noticed that the
great Pro-Consul’s korero had the accent of the Ngapuhi,
a northern tribe. Moreover, Grey has often fluently
addressed the native c hiefs without the intervention of an
interpreter. On most official occasions, however, Grey
would speak through an interpreter for the benefit of the
reporters.—Ed. B.j

J. H. YOUNG.
At Poverty Point,
Things theatrical in Coolgardie are dead—according to
Duncan M‘Galium, who has just returned looking ten
years older.
Ernest Hosking, the versatile spook-exposer, has been suc-
cessfully touring northern Victoria and Deniliquin with a
young lady illusionist as his assistant. Too much
spookery beginning to pall on the audiences, he added
ventriloquism and musical olios to his repertoire.
His assistant, described as a handsome, curly blonde,
helped the show along with clairvoyant tricks and memory
tests. She used to run the same show with Oscar Smith,
another ventriloquist.
_ Nina Boucicault, who is playing in "The Case of Rebel-
lious Susan,” at London Criterion, makes much capital
outj of the Austral Salon, Melbourne, and gives a most
amusing “take-off” of the “colonial new woman’s”
twang.
Mrs. Edouin Bryer will leave for London on an interest-
ing mission on 7th Feb. She has been playing in Aus-
tralia for upwards of 43 years, and was under engage-
ment to Coppin for 23 years. She was engaged at the
old Haymarket, Bourke-st. E., Melb.; at the Lyceum,
Lonsdale-st. (now a livery stables): and at the old Melb.
Rojal.
Although Maggie Moore was quite agreeable to play for
Mrs. Edouin Bryer’s farewell benefit, Mr. Coppin
objected. “ Because,” said he, “ Maggie Moore is
‘ billed ’ to make her farewell appearance in ‘Aladdin,’
and, therefore, I can’t allow her to play for this benefit.”
Some people aver that the veteran was annoyed because
Mrs. Bryer consulted Maggie Moore first.
Amy Roselle’s one-night performance of Lady Macbeth
with Irving’s company, is historic in the purfesh. It was
at Birmingham, where the great Henry was playing his
short annual season, that Ellen Terry became “very
seriously indisposed” and retired from the cast with the
apparent intention of resting for some considerable time.
Amy Roselle filled the breach, and her representation of
the Thane’s terrible spouse was so magnetic that it cured
Ellen’s complaint in three hours. She had no more ner-
vous attacks until a weaker "understudy ” was provided
in the Roselle’s stead.
One of the satin-attired fairies at Melb. Royal panto, is
referred to by Aladdin as “ a white spirit,” whereupon
she retorts: “Yes, I am the very best square gin 1 ”
Happily, however, Melb. is ignorant of the slang of the
far North.
The supers, on in the last scene at Melb. panto, pleaded
hard that the chill should be taken off the water into
which they are plunged. The management, however,
elevated its eyebrows at their audacity, and hinted
that the two-shilling-a-nighters ought to be very
glad of a cheap bath. This Melb. summer has, so far,
been like an average October.
Bishop Thornton, of Ballarat, wrote to the local hospital
committee expressing grave disapproval of the proposal
to ask Girly Yarmouth to skirt-dance in aid of the funds
of the institution. Some heathen suggests that the
gartered bishop is jealous of the Girl’s shanks.
The willowy Bernhardt’s latest is to wear a live falcon
on her w r rist, and a gorgeous dress of sky-blue Venetian
velvet decorated with no fewer than 4600 rubies, emeralds
and sapphires.
A London correspondent: It has been decided that
Beerbohm Tree will re-open the Haymarket with “ John-
A-Dreams,” after his return from ’Murka. He is now
turning away money—and big money, too—nightly, much
to Haddon Chambers’ delight. The play would fill
Covent Garden or Drury Lane.
It is refreshing for the two English actresses who
accompany the Dacres to hear that two others have been
“stuck ” in Melb., with nothing in their purses.
Oily Deering, as the Emperor of China, in Melb. Royal
Panto., wears 361 b. weight of padding. They now call him
Oily Deering.
The committee of Melb. Distressed Actors’ Fund, after
making necessary additions and improvements to the
Homes and putting aside sufficient to keep the inmates
for 12 months, has given away close on £4OO during the
last six months in cash to deserving and hard-up members
of the profession. The total cost of this distribution has
been £2 Bs. for advertising for applications.
The alleged shower of love-letters received by Harry
Roberts while playing juvenile leads, lovers, and “pretty”
parts, stopped dead when he began to play Widow
Twankey.
The Bulletin’s London correspondent by last mail:—
“ The Dacres sailed by the Oruba for Melbourne, where
they will join Elton in a kind of co operative theatrical
venture, under the aegis of Wilson, of the Bijou. Elton
writes hopefully of their prospects, and everybody wishes
them well, as they have been very badly treated by certain
individuals. They have a very fine repertory, including
a new play by G. R. Sims, from which they expect great
things. They take with them two pretty and clever young
ladies, better known in the Provinces than on the
London boards, who will play minor parts. I am sure
you will like the Dacres. Mrs. Dacres (Amy Roselle) is
too well-known to require any eulogy at my hands. She
is certainly one of our very best all-round actresses. Her
husband is a capital fellow and a capable actor, whose
handsome appearance alone is almost enough to carry off
any part he undertakes. Full of bonhommie, he is liked
wherever he goes. A very large party of friends
assembled at the station to wish them bon voyage and
good luck. The Savage Club mustered in full force
last night at a real “send-off” supper to Dacre.
It was one of the most pleasant evenings we have
had there for some time. Australia was well represented
by Mackennal, Alfred Dampier, J. Longstaff, Fry, of
Tasmania, Pilcher, Q. 0., of Sydney, Lett, Henniker
Heaton and others, including Phil. May, who is looked
upon as half an Australian now. Arthur Dacre, by the
way, is not alone a mummer—he is a not undistinguished
member of the medical profession, and gave up a good
practice to follow his fancy for the stage.”
Arthur Bourchier’s marriage to Miss Violet Vanbrugh,
writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent, took place
at a High church at Earl’s Court, and was quite a
smart affair. Bourchier is a “somebody” by birth,
but a wooden actor. His wife is a daughter of the late
Prebendary Barnes, of Exeter. She is a charming
daughter of a charming mother. They are both great
friends of Mrs. “Jack” Purves, at whose residence I had
the pleasure of making their acquaintance. The most
conspicuously-dressed woman at the wedding was Mrs.
Bernard Beere, who wore a gown of white serge with any
amount of braid, a most expensively-embroidered black-
velvet cloak, the whole topped by a huge hat of many
trimmings. Picture this to yourself, Sappho Smith 1
Debrett was well represented at the function.
The Daily Post, the new Sydney Labor
organ, is published at last in the face of enormous
difficulties ; and while there is nothing very bril-
liant about it thus far, it has so much room for
useful work that The Bulletin cordially hopes
the venture may succeed.
The point raised as to the illegality under
the present statute of paying N.S.W. members of
Assembly is a miserable lawyers’ quibble. If pay-
ment is not technically legal Parliament intended
it to be so and will very soon make it so. The
point is apparently to be used by Mr. Tom Slat-
tery’s friends as an advt. for his law-business.
Rumor now credits Premier Turner with
meaning to drop the land-tax altogether and re-
introduce the income-tax, with exemption to
.£2OO. This is expected to yield £200,000, which
it probably won’t ; and with £lOO,OOO more
knocked off municipal endowment, £178,000 worth
more retrenchment in the railway, postal, and
defence departments, and £35,000 gained by re-
taining the primage duties, the unfortunate
Minister hopes to make ends meet. And the Vie.
Fat Man, having successfully shifted the respon-
sibility from his own shoulders, sits and chuckles
serenely.
MTI wraith is off to England again, retain-
ing his seat in Queensland Parliament and his
place in the Ministry. For over a year he has
never set foot in the House; he has given no
assistance to the Government; yet no one has
been found to deny that he still draws his salary
regularly, without doing a tittle to earn it.
The papers which continually comment on the
greediness of Labor-members in taking pay (after
working for it) might found a profitable homily
on this.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
After more or less narrowly losing the
first two out of the five test matches, the com-
mon impression wgs that Australia would crumble
away again in the third match also, and that the
Englishmen having thus won the rubber all interest in
the proceedings would vanish there and then. Instead
of this Australia suddenly rose up and demonstrated
once more the glorious uncertainty of cricket by
winning with 382 runs to spare. Australia made
238 in the first innings and 411 in the second. Gregory
didn’t come off either time, his combined scores only
amounting to 26. In the first innings, Giffen (58), H.
Trott (48), Callaway (41), and A. E. Trott (38), were top
scorers. The second innings consisted mainly of Iredale’s
score of 140, Bruce (80), and A. E. Trott (72). The
Englishmen's first innings totalled 124—Brown (39)
and M‘Laren (25) being top scorers. Ford (21) and
Briggs and Brockwell (12 each), were the only others
who got into double figures. In their second innings
M'Laren (35), Stoddart (34) and Brockwell (24) were the
only notable features, and the rest was mostly blanks and
desolation. Under these unexpected circumstances some
sanguine Australians are already beginning to reckon on
this country winning the rubber, after all.
• • » «
The match between N.S.W. and S.A., at
Adelaide, was chiefly remarkable for the small scores, as
the wicket was first-class. When N.S.W. fell for 192 on
the Saturday several S.A. sports backed S.A. to win with
an innings to spare, and one wealthy Adelaidean lost
nearly £lOO in this way.
• ♦ « •
George Gi Sen’s average of 16 wickets for 186
runs against N.S.W. on a batsman’s wicket, is a perform-
ance without parallel in Australian first-class cricket.
Giffen clean-bowled most of his victims, including Moses,
in each innings. Moses was dubious about his form when
Giffen bowled him for 13 in the first innings, but at the
second venture he baited as well as ever, and when hs
went out Blackham, Trott and Turner worried him about
the test-match for over an hour. A. H. Jarvis’ 98, not
out, was made by beautiful cricket, marred by one
chance ; and Jarvis was very cut-up when Jones made a
wild swipe and robbed the wicket-keeper of his chance.
Jarvis has never made 100 in first-class cricket. Callaway
was the best N.S.W. bowler and got valuable wickets.
Lyons was again a comparative failure ; in fact, he seems
to be getting rapidly “ out of print.”
* ♦ • •
Cricket patriotism doesn’t seem to run wild in
Melbourne. A topical verse in the Royal panto., stating
that Australia intended to win the next three “teat”
matches, met with a rather supercilious reception last
week.
* • * •
The Englishmen back themselves in each test-
match, and some of their play suggests that they have
wagers in their eye.
* • • *
E. T. Smith wagered Lyons .£5O to nothing
he would not make 50 against N.S.W. in the first innings,
and laid him £1 to nothing about every run over that
number. Lyons made 47. The same offer was made in
the second innings. Lyons made 7.
* * ♦ •
S.A. Advertiser describes Trumper, the
N.S.W. junior, as a “ typical Comstock 1"
» * * •
It rained heavily in Adelaide two days before
the N.S.W. match, whereupon caretaker Checkett
covered the wicket with galvanised iron and canvas,
banked with sawdust, and the result was a perfect bats-
man’s wicket instead of a mudheap. Experientia docet I
♦ ♦ ♦ ♦
Walter Giffen is the mystery of S.A. cricket.
Against England he failed in both innings, but (he selec-
tion committee picked him against Vic., and he failed
again ; nevertheless, he was selected against N.S.W. to
make a third failure. Geo. Giffen is not on the selection
committee, and the S.A. public are asking why Wally is
continually picked, and good players like Bennett, Green,
and Richards overlooked.
* • ♦ *
The Australian public want to know what
particular brand of hair-dye a certain Australian crioketer
is advertising.
• • • *
Business, it was at first said, and not fear of his
leg again collapsing, precluded the ever-reliable Moses
from taking part in the third test-match. Anyhow, no
one named Moses ever lets cricket, or anything else, inter-
fere with business.
• • • •
What other game can hold a candle to cricket
for luck ? A Stanbury, a Charley Samuels, a Peter Jack-
son, or a Carbine, if “ well,” must make a good showing
against an opponent, even if beaten. If, at any fportion
of the contest, they make a mistake they have a chance
of retrieving their position ; but in cricket, if a man errs
in the first over, which he is perhaps more likely to do
than in the hundredth, he is snuffed out for good. Take
Sid. Gregory’s two innings of 201 and 2, or Stoddart’s 170
odd and las instances. At what other game would it be
possible for a first-class man to show such a woeful dis-
parity-let the weather or track be what it might?
• » * *
Is it a coincidence only that the Englishmen, in
the three test-matches, should have the worst of the deal
in the first innings ?
♦ * * *
It must much surprise the candid friends of lob-
bowler Humphreys to learn that his is the best bowling
average of the team so far in all matches (not including
the Adelaide one). His 43 wickets cost 13-15 runs apiece;
while Brockwell comes next with 14-9, and Peel (17 - 3),
Briggs (18-45), Richardson (20-13), and Lockwood (50-9)
follow as named. In eleven a-sida matches Peel is easily
first.
• • * •
Carnage is nominated for the Newmarket and
Australian Cup, which looks as though Mr. W. R. Wilson
has hopes of getting his handsome rogue to do his best
again.
• * * «
Paris is not entered for either the Newmarket
or Australian Cup, as it is really intended to ship him to
England. The people who excused Ringmaster’s defeats
by saying that he was a second-rater in Australia, cannot
say the same if Paris goes down.
* * * *
There were only seven Sydney entries for the
Australian Cup at V.R.C. Autumn Meeting. The New-
market flutter has a batch of 19 Sydneyites engaged,
Ruenalf, Pilot Boy, and Solanum being the only ones set
down for both events.
* * * ♦
Handicapper Scarr (who is finally retiring) drew
out the handicaps for last Saturday’s Warwick Farm
meeting, but asked the club secretary not to say who the
handicapper was. The handicap was duly posted up, but
without Mr. Scarr’s initials as usual. Owners and trainers
drew round and criticised the imposts, and one of the
principal trainers remarked that owners would never
stand that sort of handicapper, whoever he was. And
when Mr. Oatley informed them that that handicapper
was Mr. Scarr, there was a collapse. The little joke only
goes to show that if the man to be appointed is even the
veteran’s equal as a handicapper, the ‘‘clever ” division
intends to blackguard him as a matter of course.
One thing is very certain. The associated clubs
should fight shy of any man who is anxiously wanted,
and whose application is “ boosted ” by any remarkably
astute stable. The advocacy of the latter should at once
damn its protege’s chance.
• • * ♦
An Australian in London to The Bulletin, by
last mail: Peter Jackson donned the mittens for the first
time since his return from the States, at the Sporting
Club, last Monday evening. His opponent was David St.
John, who recently fought a game fight with a fellow-
Welshman named O’Brien (not a very Welsh name). O
course, it was merely an exercise canter for Peter, who
showed all his tine science, and seems none the worse for
his long idleness. Frank Slavin is to have a send-off ben.
at one of the big halls, when he will have a few rounds
with Joe Steers, the amateur champion. Steers is a
blacksmith, and a very line, hard-hitting boxer, but I’d
like to see George Seale, of Sydney, in the ring with him
for 20 min. Slavin is going to Johannesberg. Steers, by
the way, has not a very high opinion of the British
middle-weight boxers, and is willing to put his notion to
the test, as he offers to fight either Ted Pritchard, Craig,
“the Coffee Cooler” (so named from his dark hide), or
Ted White, the erst amateur champion, now a profes-
sional, at the Nat. Sporting Club, for any sum put up by
that institution, he to take a trophy if he wins, his
opponent to take cash. It is likely that White will accept
the gage of battle. If the affair comes off it will equal in
interest any of the fights between the big-wigs. Clark
has accepted the challenge, but the N.S.C. has a word to
say in the matter, and that word has not been spoken at
time of writing. The Coffee Cooler, aforesaid, has been
matched against Pritchard. Before the Pritchard-Burga
affair he announced that he would fight the winner, as he
did not believe in tackling either of the two men until it
proved which was the better. Altogether boxing is look-
ing up again in this effete old land.
• • * •
Rowing, says The Bulletin’s London corres-
pondent, will take a start again after the Sullivan-Hard-
ing race, as Mr. Harding is an ambitious lad, and doesn’t
mind giving a bit of weight away. If Stanbury came to
England he would find some one to tackle him I feel sure,
especially if the “ bantam ” should happen to lick Sulli-
van. He might do worse than try a trip. Surely .his
friends would raise the required funds ?

in a single innings. Seven wickets fell for one run, made
by C. Bull. The battle of Bull's Run I Obviously, the
scribes want more beef.

’ The Melb. ready-money starting price “ books ”
got a bad Jolt over the win of Pickles at Maribyrnong
last week. There were only six starters in the race, and
although the betting opened at 6 to 4 it closed at 10 to 1.
'Cos why ? No “ heads ” were backing him on the course.
He won, and the parties who backed him in town won
some hundreds at 10 to 1, but even that amount has
shaken some of the ap. merchants up so much that they
are asking time.
Latest Centralian grievance, A Rock-
hampton paper tells how a requisition was sent to
Brisbane for a dozen lead-pencils for Custom-
hpuse_ use. And the Southern authorities sent up
six with a reprimand for extravagance.
No wonder that cruel assaults by the
police upon citizens are common when the
offenders know they may wreak their rage or spite
with impunity. At Newtown (Sydney) Police-
court this week, before Mr. Giles, S.M., Francis
Young (overseer of works for the borough of
Newtown), J. Howard (contractor), T. Lincoln
(farrier), and J. Eyles (news-agent), witnesses
whose credibility is not challenged, deposed
that a constable brutally assaulted a man
named James Slade, whom he wished to
arrest. Slade’s only apparent offence was sitting
on the steps of the Town Hall at 8 a.m, ; but it is
alleged the police have “a down’’ on him, and
the constable, in arresting him, according to
the four above-named witnesses, threw Slade
down, put his knee on the back of Slade’s head,
and “rubbed Slade’s face into road-metal,”
inflicting serious wounds. The defence was the
constable’s simple denial of violence; and the
magistrate, in effect, told Slade’s four witnesses
they were liars, affirming that Slade was “ a flash
fellow and a thoroug-h scoundrel,” and had brought
the violence upon himself. Another case, heard
before the same magistrate, was just as flagrant-
looking an instance of police savagery, yet, as
usual, the magistrate sided entirely with the
constable concerned. As the citizens of Sydney
want to be able to respect the police and to assist
them in maintaining order, the authorities had
better take instant steps to prevent men with
violent tempers from wearing police-uniform and
carrying arms. It will also be necessary to
appoint to the Bench police-magistrates capable of
dispassionately weighing evidence and of giving
due value to the oaths of reputable civilians.
Holdsworfch, Macpherson & Co., the well-
known Sydney ironmongers, held a big picnic
la,st week to celebrate the opening of their splen-
did new premises in George-street, which effectu-
ally lay over anything in the ironmongery line
in Australasia. The firm dates hack as far as the
Waterloo year, and after much success and a few
vicissitudes it starts 1895 with the determination
to deserve all the patronage that the public can
give it. The firm claims its stock to be the best
and largest south of the equator.
The Empire hotel, corner of Pitt and
Hunter-streets, Sydney, has passed into the
hands of Mr. Geo. Baumann, formerly well-known
as the proprietor of Baumann’s Gate. The
Empire is a splendid caravanserai of recent date.
It has most extensive bedroom accommodation ;
the furniture is new and elegant, the ceilings
lofty, the ventilation perfect, and (here is an
abundant supply of private-rooms. Also, the
Empire is centrally situated, and has a coinph te
cellar and excellent cuisine.

OTICE OE FORECLOSURE UNDER REAL PROPERTY ACT.
In the matter of the application of William Griffin Lipscomb, transferee from Montague Parnell, the
mortgagee under memorandum of mortgage from William Green Rowe, No. 196,453, dated May
30, 1892, over the whole ®f the land comprised in Certificate of Title, vol. 1063, folio 49.
WHEREAS the abovenamed mortgagee has made application for an order for foreclosure of the
mortgage above referred to, accompanied by such evidence of default for six months and other facts, as
required by section 113 of the Real Property Act: Notice is hereby given, pursuant to the said Act,
that the land comprised within the said mortgage, as hereunder particularised, is offered and will
remain open for sale until February 22, 1895, which date has been appointed as the time upon or
after which f, the Registrar-General, will issue to the applicants an order for foreclosure, which will
exclude all claim by the abovenamed mortgagor, and of any person claiming through or under him,
unless in the interval a sufficient amount has been realised by the sale of such land to satisfy
the principal and interest moneys due, and all costs and expenses occasioned by such proceedings, and
interest up to the time of such sale.
A Plan and further particulars may be obtained by application at the Land Titles Office, Eliza-
beth-street,' Sydney.

IN Pursuance of the provisions of the “Inland
Waters Fisheries Act of 1887,” I, Sir
ROBERT WILLIAM DUFF, the Governor of
the Colony of New South Wales, with the advice
of the Executive Council, do, by this my Pro-
clamation, declare that the waters of the Edwards
River, including all affluents and tributaries
within the Counties of Wakool, Townsend, and
Cadell, shall be closed against the use of fishing-
nets for a period of one year from the date hereof.
Given under my Hand and Seal, at Govern-
ment House, Sydney, this thirty-first day
of December, in the year of our Lord one
thousand eight hundred and ninety-four,
and in the fifty-eighth year of Her
Majesty’s Reign.
By His Excellency’s Command,
JAMES N. BRUNKER.

Mechanical starting has done something towards
purifying the Australian turf. Now wanted—mechanical
jockeys ; also, mechanical judges who will never sight the
second horse first.
• • * *
Nordenfeldt is not turning out the glorious suc-
cess at the stud that his first progeny, Strathmore and
Zalinski, seemed to predict. His later “gets ” seem to be
mostly on the small sid j, and few of them have shown
anything like decent form. Nordenfeldt’s spirited
owner, Sam Hordern, valued the horse at £20,000 just
after Strathmore’s Derby. What’s his price now ?

for piles is most remarkable, large quantitie B
being sent to all parts of the colonies from the
depot at 257 George-street, Sydney.

Ed. Bulletin, —My definition ; The poet

The young King of Tonga, though only 19
years of age, is a recluse and a failure. He fears
assassination, writes a Bulletin correspondent,
a.id annexation, and his palace is guarded night
aid day. lie never goes outside the enclosure,
and two officers of guards sleep in his bedroom.
His Majesty (who spends most of his time playing
bagatelle with his secretary and coachman) was to
have prorogued the Tongan Parliament in Oct.
last. Invitations w ;re sent to the Consuls and
foreign r.-sidents, and a large crowd mustered; but
no King appeared, the speech from the throne
King read by his father, Tuibeleaki. The only
foreigner who has free access to the palace is Rev.
Jabez Watkin, his spiritual adviser.
A painter hal a Govt, contract, and was
much harried by the inspector. When the job
was completed according to specification, the
officer was a deed for a final certificate. He
pointed out that the iron spouting and pipes had
not been painted, and was referred to the specifi-
cations. Finding the items omitted, the con-
tractor was asked for a price, and said £ 5. As the
job was only worth 305., the inspector demurred,
until it was pointed out that the erection of scaf-
old cost .£lO, and if the contractor removed his,
the next man might do the job for 305., but plus
.£lO for erection of scaffolding. The painter got
the job at his own figure. Ignorance and careless-
ness cost the Government small fortunes every
year through faulty specifications; in fact,
some contractors, when they detect a flaw,
take the contract at an unrenmnerative figure,
knowing full well that their opportunity for profit
lies in the work necessary for the completion of
the job, and omitted from the specifications.
The present Victorian Premier is in every-
way different to Patterson, the meat-man. Pat-
terson found his greatest political delight in
striving to impress the ’Ouse and appal the multi-
tude. He had no sooner got used to his position
than he started to cultivate a heroic style, sticking
his left hand behind his lightly-buttoned body
and crooking his right forefinger, in imitation of
some British person as depicted by a Graphic
artist. His public life was spent in sitting or
standing for “the portrait of a famous states-
man,” and had ho not been thrown out of office
the scowling mummer would by this time have
been acting the voice of the country in a Roman
toga, or a suit of glittering cha n mail. Patterson
was, and is, an even vainer delusion than Gillies,
whose commonplace pomposity suffered from a
suspicion that his legs were too short and
straddling for the character. Turner, on the
contrary, is not a little bit of a poseur.
The axiom that “no man is a hero to his
valet” doesn’t deter Premier George from taking
a dip at Hegarty’s baths, St. Kilda. At about
(5.30 a.m. any warm morning you may see the
leader of the Government as he is—a rotund little
solicitor, with perhaps a mole or two on his plump
back. All the early bathers seem to look
kindly on Turner. There’s an air of naked
truth about him, and (being rather short-sighted)
he avoids the risk of offending a voter by saying
“How d’yer do” to everyone who comes near
him afloat or ashore. A couple of minutes in the
briny satisfies Premier T. He ducks his repre-
sentative head, swims half-a-dozen strokes, then
off for the fresh-water shower. His platform
manner is unassuming, and he doesn’t even stand
on a pedestal whilst drying himself.
“ The soldier talks of his sword, the sailor
of his guns, but the candidate never talks of his
money.”

or railway - station, make sure, before paying,
that you get the current issue, as a number of
people have been making money by trading off
ancient copies on the unsuspecting, /

It appears to be the intention of Lever
Bros, Ltd., the famous Sunlight soap manufac
turers, not only to inculcate habits of cleanliness
in our homes, but also to educate the people in
fine arts and literature. There is many a cottage,
especially in the country districts, that is adorned
with really fine copies of the best Academy pictures
given by Lover Bros., which must add a pleasant
and cheerful tone to the homo. It is difficult to
see the connection between soap and the fine arts
and literature; still, where cleanliness is sur-
rounded by the beautiful in art, the mind is more
easily educated to enjoy and benefit by reading
the works of our best writers, and Lever Bros, are
contributing to this by distributing some very
fine books as prizes, including Chambers Journal,
Sylvia’s Annual, the Art Heview, and works
by Dickens, Thackeray, Lever, &0., and to in-
augurate the New Year are giving through the
storekeeper a collection of useful Year Books,
which, dealing witli home subjects, such as home
pets, poultry, games, and amusements, the garden,
fashions and patterns, and sports, must be in-
teresting to old and young. All lovers of cricket
and cycling will rush for the book upon sports,
which contains a clever illustrated article upon
cricket, written by England’s champion, W.
Brockwell, who, as the Australians realise, can
play as well as write upon the subject. The
article by Shorthand upon cycling is also very
interesting, and the almanac is a complete com-
pendium of useful information. A serious run
upon the grocers for these books may be pro-
phesied.

A MOONLIGHT FANCY.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I have no promise but a ring,
A little band of gold ;
But that, until the lilies bring
My love to me, I’ll hold.
I’ll braid and twist my hair anew,
The way ho liked to see ;
What time the Spring comes back again
Will bring my love to me.
What time the Spring comes back again,
You foolish girl, will bring
Your love, indeed, again to you—
But not the wedding-ring.
J.M.

Exhibition of works
Of the
PHOTOGRAPHIC ART,
To be Held in the
MELBOURNE EXHIBITION GALLERIES,
From 29th JANUARY till 30th APRIL, 1895.
The Exhibition Trustees have determined to hold an
Exhibition of the Photographic Art in the Galleries con-
nected with the Aquarium, and cordially invite the
assistance and co-operation of all those who are interested
in the Photographic Art.
The Exhibition will embrace all the various branches of
the art, and will include Portraiture, Landscape, and
Scenery subjects, together with specimens of Photo-
gravure, Autotype, and all the various processes of repro-
duction of pictures from photoe-raphs, together with an
exposition of the application of Photography to Art,
Industrj', and Commerce.
A special section will .be placed at the disposal of
amateurs for the exhibition of their work, and a separate
room will be placed at the disposal of the Amateur Photo-
graphic Association of Victoria.
The Exhibition will be open on 29th January, and
remain open till April 30, 1895. All exhibits must be
furnished with an explanatory label, legibly written or
printed.
The trustees reserve the right to reject any proffered
exhibits, and to remove any objectionable labels, &0., if
necessary.
Each exhibitor of photographs, &c., of not less than
£lO in value will be furnished with a pass for entry during
the currency of the exhibition.
All exhibits must be delivered at the Exhibition-build-
ing not later than January 19, and placed as directed by
the trustees.
Entry forms and all further particulars can be obtained
on application to the undersigned.
J. E. SHERRARD,
Secretary Exhibition Trustees.

PRICE: ONE SHILLING.
One-half the world believes in its descent
from monkeys ; the other half proves it.
It is probable that at least one steamer
will leave Sydney for London next March
freighted with 750 to 1000 live cattle.
Lord Teynham is just married. Last year
a set was made at him by an Australian heiress,
who employed a retired military officer as her
broker, and the affair caused a good deal of talk.
There are enough women on the town in
Adelaide, remarks an official correspondent, to
return a member of their own class to Parliament
if they were all clustered together in one con-
stituency.
When 001. Fox, commandant of the Maori-
land forces, went to Auckland recently to inspect
the battalions, the gas-jets could not be lighted in
the drill-shed. The local commander said it was
so “ blawsted unreliable, don’tcher know; they
should have electric-light.’’ So the inspection had
to bo abandoned. Next day, the angry colonel
discovered that the gas co, had cut the drill-shed
off because its bill hadn’t been paid.
The good young man who remained alive
writes to a Maoriland paper in this strain : “ I do
not see anything about chess or chess problems in
the sacred Scriptures, and therefore I cannot
approve of them.” By-and-bye, this young man
will probably find out to his great astonishment
that there isn’t any mention of pants, or socks, or
boots, or white boiled shirts in the sacred Scrip-
tures, and then he will be arrested on suspicion of
insanity for going about with a pair of sandals on
and a sackcloth round him.
The new Ozar, by promising to release
political prisoners, and to grant a Constitution,
has practically exploded the fiction that his father
wanted to do a lot of things, but dared not on
account, of the Muscovite nobility. In point of
fact, the new Czar “gave away” his father’s
corpse before the weeping populace—who wept
under police instructions and supervision—had
completed their great national sorrow. Is is com-
monly believed, by the way, that the grandfather
of Nicholas 11. did sign a decree granting a Con-
stitution on the very day of his death—the ink
was still wet on the document when he went out
and was blown up by the Nihilists. If they had
known of the fateful document, old Alexander
might probably have sat on a powder-magazine
along with the boss-Nihilist with impunity. But
then they didn’t know.
The gentleman associated with an actress
in a small cab-smash up North, the other week,
displayed an almost pathetic anxiety to have his
name “kept out of the papers.” He was an
Englishman on a visit to the beastly kawlinies,
and he rushed round to each of the local editors
in a tall collar and a profuse perspiration, stating
that he was really “aw’fly sorry at being mixed
up in this confounded bore of an accident, don-
cherknow,” and he hoped they would say nothing
about him because of his “ people at ‘home,’
bai jove !” The press respected the poor fellow’s
feelings, and preserved his incognito in the matter.
He was not much more than 40 years of age,
consequently it would have been most unkind to
alarm the family, who might have reduced his
pocket-money. A doosid narrow escape, though.
The English gentleman couldn’t afford to risk
any more friendship with an. actress who has cab
accidents, dash it all I and when last heard of he
was aboard a P. and O. steamer, returning to his
“people.”

For some time back the North Canterbury
(M.L.) Education Board has ukased that no girl
over 12 shall be caned in any of the district
schools, but recently this order has been rescinded,
and young ladies of any age can now be whaled
in case of need. Experience proved that the very
bad girl of the school, on her twelfth birthday,
would commence making faces at the master, and
put out her tongue at him, and otherwise knock
discipline into a cocked hat. The miserable
teacher had no redress save to “ keep her in,” and
she generally refused to stay in ; or to expel her,
whereupon the truant officer would order her to
be re-admitted. In almost every large school
there was at least one bad girl who kept the
establishment in a state of riot. Now the in-
furiated teacher, after months of worry and
smothered indignation, has at last a chance to get
level with that young lady of a little over 12,

Our Trade is Big I Our Store is Big J
Our Ideas are Big!
We get bigger every day; on Saturday niglit
we are bigger than on the previous Monday,
and on the Wednesday following we are bigger
still. We can’t help growing. We have all the
elements of growth. The soil-we afe planted in
is good, the climate propitious, the people kind.
We are continually watered „by the lifegiving
showers of a nation’s approval. Can anyone
wonder that we grow ?
Our growth is a wonderful thing. People
describe it as magical. There is no magic in it.
Anyone, or any set of people, can do the same if
they do as we have done. But they don’t; they
can’t find the way, charge too much for the privi-
lege of doing business with them, and generally
and deplorably make a the whole affair.
That’s the mischief.
Most People Like Us I All Believe in Us!
Not a Few Swear by Us I
It’s cupboard love. We suit ;them, make their
money go a long way, and generally render the
path of life an easy one. There’s no sentiment
about it. It’s real biz, and no' mistake. In that
way we are beloved throughput Australia, the
measure of affection each one bestows upon us
being gauged by the amount of benefit received.
The more you buy at our store the greater your
liking for us will become, because the more you
invest with us the better dividends you receive.
If living IN THE COUNTRY, stfiid for our
Catalogue and Patterns of anything you require ;
if in town, call every day.

SOMETHING ABOUT TWO BOYS.
“The most pathetic incident of my childhood is
this : My mother had been very ill for several
weeks, and the doctor solemnly announced that
she could not live more than two or three days
longer at most. That night my father roused me
from sleep and took me out of my little bed to bid
her a last good-bye. I shall never forget the
scene, which was new and awful to me. People
were weeping all round the room, the air of which
was heavy with the odour of candles and lamps,
and reeking with the fumes of drugs. My mother
knew and kissed me, and then they took me hack
to my bed. But ere I was led away some one
opened the window a few inches from the top, and
I noticed the grey dawn resting on the glass, and
heard the ‘cheep, cheep,’ of a newly-awakened
bird. Since then I have associated that hour and
sound with that unhappy episode.
“But (and to say what now follows I have
written the foregoing paragraph) we were all
rasped and tortured for nothing. My mother
proceeded to get well hand-over-hand, and died
quietly thirty years afterwards. She survived
every person who stood at her bedside that night,
except me.”
(Speaking of the illness of her son, a boy of
nine, a lady says : “We had to sit with him
night and day, giving him brandy, wine, beef-
tea, &c., to keep him alive, and expected every
day would be his last. The physician plainly told
us that nothing more could be done to save him.’’
Yet, in spite of the disease, and—we almost
said—in spite of the doctors, the lad is well to-day.
And this is how it all came about. There is a
moral in it, too, but suppose we serve that up at
the end of the story. All right, you say. Very
well, then.
It seems that this boy, George Westmoreland,
had previously been a strong, healthy little chap,
as all boys ought to be. But about the middle of
last November—lB9l, that is—he was taken
down. The family couldn’t make out what ailed
him. He complained of a bad pain in the stomach,
and vomited a quantity of yellowish-green stuff.
Presently the pain was so sharp he couldn’t lie in
bed, and they had, so his mother says, to apply
fresh hot poultices one after another. The whites
of his eyes turned yellow, and his akin, too. He was
hot, and feverish, and had to fight for his breath.
Of course his mother sent, for a doctor, and the
doctor said his young patient was suffering from
inflammation of the bowels. He gave medicines
which, however, did no'good, so far as the boy’s
friends could see. On the contrary, he grew
worse, and a second doctor was fetched. This
medical gentleman differed from his predecessor,
and gave out that George had an attack of rheu-
matic fever—in other words, acute rheumatism —
a disease which no boy has any business with
whatever.
The treatment on this theory availed nothing;
George was worse. He now had a hacking cough,
and his expectoration was so offensive that the
people had to use disinfectants. He broke out
into sweats, so as to saturate the pillows.
He could take no nourishment save a little milk
and lime water. He wore away to a skeleton, did
the poor boy. He was nothing but skin and bone,
and they had to lift him in and out of bed. Then
he fell so ill he would not notice anyone in the
room, and lay for hours never opening his eyes.
Then came the time when a third doctor said he
couldn’t possibly live.
What happened after that the boy’s mother
tells. We give you her exact words: “In
February last,” she says, “ my husband, as a last
resource, determined to try Mother Seigel’s Cura-
tive Syrup. After a few doses the boy’s breathing
was easier and he took food. In three days he
was able to sit up, and in a week’s time he was up
and dressed. He gained flesh and strength every
day, and is now able to go about. Sometimes I
look at him and can hardly believe he is the same
boy who was so recently at death’s door. Seigel’s
Syrup saved his life. Yours truly, (signed) Mrs.
Mary Westmoreland, 5, High Street, Plumstead,
London, April 27, 1892.”
Now, half a dozen words. Little George had
no bowel inflammation, nor a single touch of rheu-
matism. That was the doctors’ professional guess-
work. He had a sharp attack of biliousness and
indigestion, of which Mother Seigel would have
cured him long before had her medicine been
appealed to. Here is the moral to conclude with:
Learn what the true remedy for illness is,, and use
it first instead of last.

Until you get into the inner circle of the London
company-promoting world, writes a well-informed A iglo-
Australian to Tub Bulletin, you have no conception
of the depth of swindling there or the extent to which it
is encouraged by prominent and “ respectable ” men,
only provided there is “ bunce ” enough. The life of a
promoter, when successful, is only a short one. Unsuc-
cessful promoters may live long. Success in landing the
public “properly” may mean 12 months’ working in one’s
own name ; after that in somebody else’s ; so a new man
never knows who is pulling the strings when he is inter-
viewing an ostensible promoter. You can never find a
man who runs his business in his own name for long if he
be at all prominent.
• ♦ ♦ ♦
Stockbroker Meudell, of Melbourne, writes to
the Financial News an expos 6 of the greed of London
promoters in floating Westraliary mines. How matters
stand in many of the recent floats .the public will never
know. As some of the mines are valueless and were
obtained for next to nothing, it is fair to guess
that the promoters would take five-sixths of the profit in
cash and shares, and that more money was spent in adver-
tising than will ever be spent in the mines. The London
mine-promoter is a marvellous personage. He takes little
or no risk, and has no right of ownership ; if the public
fail to put up his £lO,OOO profit he declines to pay the
owners, say, £lOOO for their mine. He is an agent who
charges 500 per cent, commission. In the Londonderry
case, the public knew that five-sixths of this mine had
been bonded tor £170,000 by the payment of £lO,OOO
deposit, and are alleged to have taken it at about four
times that price. Who gets the difference? The pro-
moters, who had taken a risk of £lO,OOO, and who, had
they failed to float at £700,000, would probably have been
backward when the day for the payment of the £160,000
arrived.
* * * *
During a public examination in London in con-
nection with the African Landed Estates Co. it transpired
that the “ estates” had been bought for £lOOO, and sold
to the miserable shareholders of the co. for £400,000.
Considering the fatherly lectures on financial morality
which are perpetually cabled out from Hingland to Aus-
tralia, this sort of thing is interesting. With such a
record as hers the good old mother-country ought to lie
low and say as little as possible.
* * • •
The Wesfcralian gold-boom in London has been
overshadowed by the great activity in South African gold
stocks, and even London cannot boom two fields at once.
Last advices say London was mad on deep-levels, and the
S. A. boom was at high level.

D. G. M‘Fie, who originated and is now manag-
ing director of the N. Queensland Mines Agency (Ltd.), at
one time ran a little mining paper in Croydon and Bris-
bane, but could get no chance to spread himself. D. G.
is a man of big ideas. He goes on the principle that it is
as easy to engineer a big scheme as a little one, and this
half-year he is giving the Britishers an interim div. at the
rate of 25 per cent, per annum.

* * • »
The good ore in Mt. Drysdale consists of a pipe-
shoot about 40ft. long, and varying in thickness from a
few inches near the surface to several feet at 125 ft.
Quartz-veins occur occasionally, but are barren. Chloride
of silver is associated with the gold, and the rich ore can-
not be distinguished from the barren country rock.
Nevertheless, this geological puzzle last year paid £22,784
diva, from 2300 tons.

provide £1 for £l, and the proceeds to be spent in work-

N.S.W. insolvency records give 17 boarding-
house-keepers and 81 hotel-keepers as victims of last year.
These, as a rule, are the lines of life most directly affected
by general prosperity and adversity, and the figures
quoted support the theory that the past was a “ bad ”
year.

of the Glen Wills field, working an auriferous dyke of
great width, from which about 1000 tons of half-oz. ore
have been crushed. Recent tunnel-explorations at a
deeper level disclose a lode 18ft. wide, assaying about
an oz.

A vendor of gold-field condensers has a tap
of pure water running into the tank of the article,
so that when a customer comes in and witnesses
the slimiest and saltest filth imaginable poured into
the top, he very naturally imagines that the crystal
fluid issuing from the apparatus three minutes later
is the same liquid in a purified condition ; whereas the
smellful stuff is really at that time floating peacefully
gutterwards through an underground drain connected
with the hollow leg of the condenser.
The grip patent (for which .£3OOO was paid),
used on the Ocean-st. (Sydney) cable tram-line, having
proved defective, a local manufacturing firm took the
matter in hand, and patched the grips up so that they
are now quite serviceable. The n<-xt difficulty was the
warning-bell, and experiments have been s;oing on for
several weeks with a view to ascertaining the particular
sortof hell best suited to the traffic ; consequently almost
every dummy has a It'd! of different tone and shape,
which ding-dong a perfect jargon of misery to nervous
people. In the end it is believed the bell to be adopted
will be in shape something like a policeman’s helmet, and
will emit a sound quite unlike any other street-noise.

Bradford (Eng.) manufacturers complain, on dit,
that Australian wool is reaching them in bad order, being
knotted, torn and tangled. They are inclined to blame
machine-shearing for this, but this view is hardly borne out
by facts. The real culprit is the festive “blackleg”
shearer, who takes off wool, skin, and a good slice of mut-
ton as well. In the hands of the “blackleg” the machine
becomes a murderous instrument of torture—as the poor
sheep find. Now the torture seems to be extending to the
squatters themselves. - .
• • • •
Brisbane (Q.) Courier urged a reduction of
shearing rates, as an appropriate comment on a Brisbane
conference of squatters. Which means that the Q.
squatter means to run another strike in ’95.

Steelman.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Steelman was a hard case. If you were
married, and, settled down, and were so unfor-
tunate as to have known Steelman in other days,
he would, if in your neighborhood and dead-beat,’
be pretty sure to look you up. He’d find you any-
where, no matter what precautions you might
take. If he came to your house, he’d stay to tea
without invitation ; and, if he stayed to tea, he’d
ask you to “ fix up a shakedown on the floor, old
man,” and put him up for the night; and, if he
stopped all night, he’d remain—well, until some-
thing better turned up.
There was no shaking off Steelman. Also, he
had a way about him which would often make it
appear <ts if you invited him to stay, and pressed
him against his roving inclination, and were glad
to have him round for company, while he remained
only out of pure goodwill to you. He didn’t like
to offend an old friend by refusing his invitation.
* * *
Steelman knew his men.
The married victim generally had neither the
courage nor the ability to turn 'him out. He was
cheerfully blind and deaf to all hints, and if the
exasperated “missis” “said anything” to him,
“straight,” he would look shocked and reply, as
likely as not—
“ Why, my good woman, you must be mad !
I’m your husband’s guest!”
And if she wouldn’t cook for him, he’d cook for
himself.
* * #
There was no choking him off. Few
people care to call the police in a case like this ;
and, besides, as before remarked, Steelman knew
his men. The only way to escape from him was
to shift—but then, as likely as not, he’d help pack
up and come along with his portmanteau right on
top of the last load of furniture, and drive you
and your wife to the verge of madness by the calm
style in which he proceeded to superintend the
hanging of your pictures.
* * #
Once he quartered himself like this on an
old schoolmate of his named Brown, who had got
married and steady and settled down. Brown
tried all ways to get rid of Steelman, but he
couldn’t do it. One day Brown said to Steelman :
6 Look here, Steely, old man, I’m very
sorry, but I’m afraid we won’t be able to accom-
modate you any longer—to make you comfortable,
I mean. You see, a sister of the missis is coming
down on a visit for a month or two, and we ain’t
got anywhere to put her except in your room. I
wish the missis’s relations to blazes ! I didn’t
marry the whole blessed family ; but it seems I’ve
got to keep them.”
Pause—very awkward and painful for poor
Brown. Discouraging silence from Steelman.
Brown rested his elbows on his knees, and, with a
pathetic and appealing movement of his hand
across his forehead, he continued desperately:
“I’m very sorry, you see, old man—you know
I’d like you to stay—l want you to stay. . , It
isn’t my fault—it’s the missis’ doings. I’ve done
my best with her, but I can’t help it. I’ve been
more like a master in my own house —more com-
fortable—and I’ve been better treated since I’ve
had you to back me up. . . I’ll feel mighty
lonely, anyway, when you’re gone. . . But . .
you know . . as soon as her sister goes . .
you know
Here poor Brown broke down—very sorry he
had spoken at all; but Steely came to the rescue
with a ray of light.
“ What’s the matter with the little room at the
back ? ” he asked.
“ Oh, we couldn’t think of putting you there,”
said Brown with a last effort; “it’s not fixed up ;
you wouldn’t be comfortable, and, besides, it’s
damp, and you’d catch your death of cold. It was
never meant for anything but a wash-house. I’m
sorry I didn’t get another room built on to the
house.”
“ Bosh ! ” Interrupted Steelman, cheerfully.
“ Catch a cold ! Here I’ve been knocking about
the country for the last five years—sleeping out in
all weathers—and do you think a little damp is
going to hurt me ? Booh ! What do you take me
for ? Don’t you bother your head about it any
more, old man; I’ll fix up the lumber-room for
myself, all right; and all you’ve got to do is to let
me know when the sister-in-law business is
coming on, and I’ll shift out of my room in time
for the missis to get it ready for her. Here, have
you got a bob on you ? I’ll go out and get some
beer. A drop’ll do you good.”
“Well, if you can make yourself comfortable,
I’ll be only too glad for you to stay,” said the
utterly defeated Brown.
“You’d better invite some woman you know to
come on a visit, and pass her off as your sister,”
said Brown to his wife, while Steelman was gone
for the beer. “ I’ve made a mess of it.”
Steelman knew his men.
» * *
But, at last, Brown reckoned that he
could stand it no longer. The thought of it made
him so wild that he couldn’t work. He took a
day off to get thoroughly worked up in, came
home that night full to the chin of indignation
and Dunedin beer, and tried to kick Steelman
out. And Steelman “stoushed him”—gave him
a hiding.
* * *
Next morning Steelman was sitting beside
Brown’s bed with a saucer of vinegar, some brown
paper, a raw beef-steak, and a bottle of soda.
“Well, what have you got to say for yourself
now, Brown ?” he said, sternly, “Ain’t you jolly
well ashamed of yourself to come home in the
beastly state you did last night, and insult a guest
in your house, bo say nothing of an old friend—
and perhaps the best friend you ever had, if you
only knew it? Anybody else would have given
you in charge and got you three months for the
assault. You ought to have some consideration
for your wife and children, and your own character
—even if you haven’t any for your old mate’s feel-
ings. Here, drink this, and let me fix you up a
bit; the missis has got the breakfast waiting.”
* # #
That was five years ago, and Steelman is
still living with the Browns.

A Newcastle (N.S.W.) constable is named
Fullalove. They generally are.
The debt of Westralia is now £4l per
head of the population, and, despite the fact that
population nearly doubled in ’93, the exports for
that year were considerably less than in ’92.
The most reliable export of Westralia is stone-
broke diggers from Coolgardie and the Murchison.
If the gold-boom bursts badly the wild-cat pro-
vince will be in a worse state than any of its
neighbours.

The Fire at Thompson’s Ford,
A VICTORIAN SHEARING EPISODE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Hottest pf hob December’s days.
Fierce and strong are the sun’s keen rays ;
The north wind’s sulphurous breath blows strong;
Even the magpie stills his song.
Over the pools of the stagnant creek
Steaming hangs the vaporous reek.
Under the scanty she-oaks crowd
Panting cattle, breathing loud.
Slowly, out in the eastern sky,
A wreath of smoke climbs clear and high.
It sways in the air like a tangled cord—
A fire has started at Thompson’s Ford !
Thompson’s shed is late this year,
Most of his sheep are yet to shear ;
“ Blackleu” labor, with half a board —■
No “truck ” with fhe Union at Thompson’s ford-
- The Union camp breaks up to day ;
Horses are saddled and ready, but stay !
Thompson shears with “ scabs.” Just so.
But a big bush-fire is a common foe.
Down the old gully, along the flat,
Red Bill leading with one-eyed Mat ;
Past the stockyard, over the brush,
Mat now first, the rest in a crush ;
Seven long miles in a blazing sun,
And yet the prelude has but begun.
The flames in mountains roar and swirl;
Dense, black smoke-clouds over them curl;
Bounding, crackling, leaping higher—
Ah, strong men shrink from a big bush fire !
“ A real old bender,” says Mat, “ Good Lord !
But we’ve got to stop her at Thompson’s ford,”
The river at Thompson’s ford runs low —
“ But the homestead goes if it’s crossed, you know. ”
Yes, that was a battle, and, even now,
The very thought just pains my brow.
Choked with smoke, dry as a board,
With our teeth hard-set we held the ford.
Side by side in that desperate band,
Squatter and shearer, hand-to-hand,
Fought like tigers for hour on hour,
Never a man was seen to cower.
The sun in the west began to sink,
When, choking, panting, gasping for drink,
We sent up a cheer, aye, how we roared—
We had stopped the tire at Thompson’s ford !
Next year, Thompson’s notice states :
“Shearers wanted, union rates.”

" On the Land.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Hot!
A blistering heat, that drove the fowls, with
their beaks gaping, off the burning ground into
the shelter of the slab hut. A scorching, wither-
ing heat, that had made sapless hay of the grass
in the paddocks, and reddened the foliage of the
gums and the stringy-barks like the blaze of a
bush-fire. A heat that had sucked up all moisture,
and marbled the land with cracks of shrink-
age, and turned the waterholes into mere mud-
baths ; while the distant creek had nothing but
its brown flag-grass and dry swamp bed to show
where the water had run.
About the homestead, the few cattle still alive
hung with their heads stolidly over the slip-rails,
refusing, in spite of all driving, to seek again for
the sustenance it was impossible to find. The
framework of a horse, his head between his legs,
and as much in shade as he could get on the lee
side of an old ironbark, stood impassive and
motionless. All round the horizon, a dirty haze
of smoke that melted into a yellow veil of fog
covering the sky.
The sun glared down. The air shimmered
tremulously, as though it sickened with its burden.
And over all that land not one gleam of freshness,
not one sound of joy, not a murmur save the
eternal zing-zing-zing of the locust. Seared,
blasted, stricken, a curse was upon it and upon
every living thing.
Abe Saunders was down at what used to be the
creek, trying to drag out his last milking cow
from the place when she had bogged in a vain
effort to find water-grass.
Abe hauled and strained at the rope, his shirt
and trousers clinging to him with sweat. The beast
kicked and plunged feebly, its little strength
quite gone ; and after two hours’ work it was more
firmly bogged than ever.
“ It’s no good,” said Abe, “ I must get help;
I’ll go up the gully for old Mason and his tackle.
If we lose that cow, what will the children do for
milk ? ”
Wearily he turned to go, when the sound of a
“ Coo-ee ! ” turned him sharply about.
“Coo-ee ! ” he shouted.
A little pause, and again the cry echoed.
“Coo-ee!”
“ It’s Mary,” he said ; “what the devil’s up?”
It was a good three miles to the ridge ; but, like
a wallaby with the dogs in full cry, he sped
over rock and gully back to the homestead. Long
before he got there, he heard a strange roaring in
the air, saw black, belching clouds over the tree
tops, and felt a fierce rushing furnace breath.
The bush was on fire.
With the energy of fear he dashed along. There
was the clearing, with Mary at the house-door
still shouting at intervals. Even as he came up,
the red storm was upon them. In mad frenzy he
seized the two children, one under each arm, and
shrieking to his wife above the din of the fire to
bring the baby, he rushed to the centre of the
ploughed paddock. There they crouched panting.
The children were howling, the baby was crying.
and Mary was sobbing. The man said nothing,
He watched the fire.
Would the house escape ? There was fifty feet
of bare ground all about it. But the air, so calm
a minute ago, was now a roaring hurricane travel-
ling at racing speed over the ridge. The tea-tree
scrub melted before it, and the bush trees remained
in its rear only as black and burning trunks. The
fence had caught; the flames licked it up daintily,
The shed, with his cart and harness, were, and
were not, while he looked. A piece of burning
stringy-bark, whirled by the wind, settled on the
shingles of the house. The woman moaned and
pressed the child closer to her bosom. The man’s
face was drawn in agony.
Week by week, day by day, he had seen the
work of his life perish under the merciless sun
The best of his cattle were festering carcases; his
horses were dead, though his last pound had been
spent in hay for them. His season’s crop was
sickly beyond redemption. All this he had borne,
and still toiled in the unequal struggle.
And now the house was burning, the work of
his own hands. How many months of weary
sawing and splitting had its shingles and slabs
cost him ? He thought of it all as he stood there,
helpless and half-suffocated. In less than a minute
the flames were shooting out of the doorways and
windows, _ and a loud report was followed by the
fall of a side of the house. It was the explosion
of his powder-flask, hung on the wall.
“ Can’t you put it out, Abe ?”
“ Put but hell!” And he ground his teeth.
another. The spirit of the man revolted.
, u ~ u • j . v . «
T L ? Mary,’.’he cned, ‘ there goes the cradle
I made for the kids. And as he spoke t e as-
pect of his face changed. The limit of his suffer-
mg had come, and, like an old-time victim of t o
rack, he began to laugh. A hollow laugh, weir
an <.mu rr i. e * j• i ht , mu r , ,
That s a good joke, Mary . The farce is ended
f. Ve > r in on f a< A fc u' » a ’- j u■ a 'e ,* , .
You re mad, Abe, said his wife, shrinking
from him, with a great dread in her eyes. Don t
that. It s horrible , .
“Mad, my dear! That s good. Ha, ha, ha.!
Say I’ve been mad—the most confounded lunatic
in this blasted, blistering country. To slog and
belt for ten long years to make a home of our own;
to clear land, to fence it, drain it, plant it—and
all to make five minutes’ bonfire ? Yes, I’ve been
mad, stark staring mad ; but now-ha, ha, ha I
I was never so sensible in my life !
“See how the cradle burns, Mary. It was a
bit of she-oak, and worked like a watch. Don’t
it look pretty now ?—they might be silk curtains,
all those flounces round it! Why don’t you laugh.
girl?—it’s a great joke. Look ! the roof is falling
in. It’s as good as fireworks. Hold up the kids ;
let them laugh. It’s all the same price. Ha, ha,
ha ! ”
But the woman replied nothing, frightened,
staring at him. The children screamed.
The driving sheet of flame had long gone by.
Skirting the ploughed ground where they stood, it
had left the bare surface an untouched blank in
its ghastly funeral trail. Only tree-stumps, posts,
and fallen branches, smoked and smouldered here
and there. The man stood motionless till nothing
but the four charred corner-posts remained of his
home.
“ Let’s see the play out,” he said. “ Ha, ha,
ha! Ha, ha, ha!”
The fire was raging many miles further onward
when Abe Saunders, taking the two children by
the hand, led the way down the track to the main
road. His wife dragged wearily after him, the baby
in her arms, looking back from time to time at the
smoking ruin.
The group passed on to the road, where the red
dust blew in thick choking clouds that shrouded
them from view. But long after they had dis-
appeared there sounded the wail of the children.
the moaning of the woman, and loud above all the
mirthless laughter of a broken-hearted man.
HENRY FLETCHER.
Quote : “A miss with a mission is usually
after a man with a mansion.”
Names and occupations. In Victoria are —
tailor. Suitor; butcher, Al-lard; undertakers,
Dark and Skelton.
The Victorian Land-tax will not he applied
to the closed roads. The rich man, who owns the
adjacent* property, gently fences in abandoned
roads, but he doesn’t pay for them, and he isn’t
charged rent for them because he only holds them
in trust for the State, and he won’t pay land-tax
on them because they are not his own. By-and-
bye, however, the fact that they are public pro-
perty gets lost in the gloom of antiquity, and he
keeps them altogether. At £1 an acre there are
over .£lOO,OOO waiting to be collected as the price of
abandoned roads which the Fat Person has
quietly absorbed.
This is one of the things which make men
believe in Providence. A few weeks ago a gang
of eight men were cutting a road over a huge cliff
at Rangitikei (M.L.). The cook’s clock, by some
unaccountable process, gained 15 minutes during
the forenoon, and he called the men to dinner at
a quarter to one o’clock. About ten minutes to
one a dozen tons of rock fell on the spot where
they had been working, and buried all their tools
beneath the ruins. And now eight men firmly
believe that Providence came in silently while the
cook wasn’t looking and shoved the clock ahead
to save their lives —which could have been saved
quite as easily by postponing the fall of that rook
until 1.5 p.m.

Begin 1895 well. Readers of The Bulle-
tin are reminded that Hordern Brothers, 203-11

Nobby noble Earl of 8.,
What they cabled o’er the sea
Was hardly worth the sending out so far :
For the party that you join,
Be it rich, or short of coin,
Cannot matter to the party that you are.
You’ve a coronet and gown
Trimmed with ermine up and down,
And, no matter what it pleases you to say,
By an accident of birth
You’ve a title upon earth
Which will always give a Liberal away.
